Abstract
Background
Intracranial Pressure (ICP) is the pressure within the skull, is measured in mmHg, and
provides valuable information about the perfusion of the brain. ICP monitoring is daily
routine in neurosurgical and neurointensive care practice for both acute and long-term
patients, and is used for treatment planning or diagnosis.
Manual assessment is time
consuming and very subjective to each neurologist, introducing great variance of inter-
pretation. ICP pattern has been described as A-, B- and C- waves with division of B-wave
into three sub-types. No attempts have been found using machine learning for pattern
recognition in ICP signals.
Objective
The objectives of this thesis includes; Developing a data acquisition software tool intended
for manual annotation of ICP curves; Proposing a classiﬁcation protocol aimed to improve
ICP pattern assessment; Applying advanced machine learning on acquired annotated data
to automatically analyze ICP signals for pattern recognition.
Methods
ICP Data was acquired through a developed software system designed for manual annota-
tion. Two neurologists from the Clinic of Neurosurgery, Copenhagen University Hospital,
independently scored a total of 11 and 6 patients providing approx. 170 and 94 hours
respectively with a sample frequency of 5 Hz. Classiﬁcation protocol was composed to
assist in annotations of six ICP wave patterns.
Identiﬁed as normal (Solid), varying
non-repetitive (Irregular), continuous repetitive (Regular B), smaller A-wave appearance
(small A), classic A-wave (A-waves) and larger A-wave deﬂections (Large-A-Like). Fea-
tures were generated on 5 min epochs with 75% overlap on Standardized and Raw ICP
data. Multi-class Support Vector Machine (SVM) using the one-vs-rest approach was de-
signed to train and test on annotated ICP data. Best features for individual classes was
found using sequential forward features selection with Bhattacharyya distance as separ-
ability measure.
Results
SVM classiﬁcation performed poorly on both Standardized and Raw ICP data. Mean
F1score of 43.4 ± 1.77% and 44.4 ± 3.27% respectively, and Accuracy of 42.4 ± 1.45% and
36.3 ± 2.9%. No signiﬁcant diﬀerence was found between F1score (p=0.67) and Accuracy
iii
==PAGE:6==
iv
ABSTRACT
(p=0.051).
Agreement between neurologists was found to be 66.6 ± 10.2%, with the
Irregular class having a TPR of 41.8 ± 34.6% and PPV of 68.9 ± 34.5% being the worst.
A-waves were better classiﬁed with a TPR of 75.0 ± 46.3% and PPV of 96.9 ± 8.8%.
Conclusion
The software platform ICP Registration and Classiﬁcation System (ICP-RCS) was suc-
cessfully implemented at the Clinic for manual annotation of ICP patterns. ICP patterns
described in a classiﬁcation protocol is composed for future standardization. SVM clas-
siﬁcation had poor performance speciﬁcally to rare class cases. No gain was found by
standardizing ICP data prior to classiﬁcation, only indications (p=0.051). Enhancement
of intra- and interscore variability between neurologists must be initiated for improved
SVM performance. The weakly described Irregular class must be revised for further im-
provement.
==PAGE:7==
Resume
Baggrund
Intrakranielt Tryk (IKT) er bogstavligt talt trykket inde i hovedet målt i mmHg. Det
angiver vigtig information om hjernens perfusion. IKT monitorering er daglig rutine i
neurokirurgiske og neurointensive afdelinger for både akutte og langvarige patienter og er
brugt til behandlingsplanlægning og diagnosticering. Manuel vurdering af IKT kurver er
tidskrævende og meget subjektiv for hver enkelte neurolog, hvilket introducerer en stor
variation i tolkningen. IKT mønstre er blevet beskrevet som A-, B- og C- bølger med
en underinddeling af B-bølgerne i tre typer. Der er ikke fundet noget forsøg på at bruge
maskinelæring til mønstergenkendelse af IKT signaler.
Formål
Formålene i dette speciale inkluderer at udvikle et programmel data opsamlingsværktøj til
manuel annotering af ICP kurver, foreslå en klassiﬁkation protokol designet til at forøge
vurdering af IKT mønstre og anvende avanceret maskinelæring på opsamlet annoteret
data til automatisk at analysere IKT signal med henblik på mønstergenkendelse.
Metoder
IKT data er opsamlet via det udviklede software system, som er designet til manuel anno-
tering. To neurologer fra Neurokirurgisk Afdeling på København Universitets Hospital har
individuelt scoret 11 og 6 patienter, hvilket giver ca. 170 of 94 timers scoret IKT data ved
en sampling frekvens på 5 Hz. Klassiﬁkations protokollen er sammensat til at assistere i
annoteringen af de seks IKT-bølgemønstre. Protokollen indeholder beskrivelser af normal
ICP mønster (Solid), varierende ikke-gentagende (Irregulært), kontinuerligt gentagende
(Regulære B), små A-bølge udseende (lille A), klassisk A (A-bølge) og store A lignende
afbøjninger (store A-lignende). Feature er genereret på 5 mins epoker med et overlap på
75% på standardiseret og rå IKT signaler. Multi-klasse Support Vector Machine (SVM),
hvor en-mod-alle metoden er brugt til at træne og teste på annoteret IKT data. Bedste
features for hver enkelte klasse er valgt ved at bruge sekventiel fremad feature selektion
med Bhattacharyya’s afstand som separations mål.
Resultater
SVM klassiﬁkationen viste utilfredsstillende resultater på både standardiseret og rå IKT
data. Gennemsnitlig F1score var hhv. 43.4 ± 1.77% og 44.4 ± 3.27%, samt 42.4 ± 1.45%
og 36.3 ± 2.9% for nøjagtighed.
Ingen signiﬁkant forskel blev fundet mellem F1score
v
==PAGE:8==
vi
RESUME
(p=0.67) og nøjagtighed (p=0.051). 66.6 ± 10.2% overensstemmelse mellem neurologerne
blev fundet, hvor Irregulær klassen var den svageste med en TPR på 41.8±34.6% og PPV
på 68.9 ± 34.5%. A-bølger var bedre klassiﬁceret med en TPR på 75.0 ± 46.3% og PPV
på 96.9 ± 8.8%.
Konklusion
Software systemet ICP-RCS blev implementeret på afdelingen og brugt til manuelt at
annotere IKT mønstre. IKT mønstre beskrevet i klassiﬁkationsprotokollen er udarbej-
det til fremtidig standardisering. SVM klassiﬁkation viste ringe resultater, specielt ved
underrepræsenterede klasser. Ingen fordele var funde ved at standardisere IKT data før
klassiﬁcering - kun indikationer (p=0.051). Forbedring af intra- og interscore variabilitet
mellem neurologerne skal påbegyndes for at forhøje SVM klassiﬁkations evne. Den svagt
beskrevet Irregulære klasse skal revideres for yderlige forbedring.
==PAGE:9==
Preface
This thesis is written as the ﬁnishing project and part of the requirements to obtain the
Master of Science degree in the ﬁeld of biomedical engineering at the Technical University
of Denmark(DTU). The thesis is completed in collaboration with the Clinic of Neurosur-
gery, Copenhagen University Hospital and is nominated to 30 ECTS points. The project
presents a new approach to a small researched area regarding wave patterns in Intracra-
nial Pressure (ICP) recordings and is divided into two main phases; 1) Concerning the
composition of classiﬁcation protocol, gathering of annotated ICP patterns and software
development with the Clinic of Neurosurgery. 2) Regards data analysis, signal processing
and machine learning on the acquired ICP data in Part I. All software system and signal
processing was developed in MATLAB ®

==PAGE:17==
Chapter 1
Introduction
Intracranial Pressure (ICP) is an important vital sign alongside electrocardiography (ECG),
heart rate (HR) and blood pressure (BP) providing vital information about perfusion of
the brain [42]. Continuous monitoring provides essential information of intracranial patho-
logy and on-going critical changes in the intracranial haemodynamics [28]. A mean ICP
value for a healthy adult patient in horizontal (supine) position is in the range of 7–15
mmHg, but can vary according to patient’s age, position, and clinical condition [2].
Measuring the ICP during and post-operational began in the 1960’s with Nils Lundberg
as the pioneer. Lundberg observed and described unique pressure wave patterns in long-
term ICP monitoring and proved the importance and signiﬁcance of this procedure in
patients with severe brain conditions [36]. By Lundbergs description, ICP patterns were
identiﬁed as A-, B- and C- waves and widely accepted until Yokata et al. [52] further
divided the B-waves in three sub-types, which are now used in clinics. ICP monitoring is
fundamental to the management of numerous pathologies ranging from Traumatic Brain
Injury (TBI), Subarachnoid Hemorrhage (SAH) to Hydrocephalus, Idiopathic Intracranial
Hypertension (IIH) or other conditions causing changes to the Intracranial Volume (ICV)
[46].
Today ICP monitoring is daily routine in neurosurgical and neurointensive care prac-
tice for both acute and long-term patients [4]. ICP data is acquired trough a small surgical
intervention where a probe is placed within the brain cavity for several hours or days [2].
The ICP curve is manually assessed by the neurologists, which is a time consuming pro-
cess, with software providing little additional information. Recently, home monitoring of
ICP is more frequently performed in clinics as a safe alternative to in-hospital [3]. In-
creasing the amount of accessible data calls for modern computerized analysis tools to
help decrease neurologist workload and keep their high performance. Most literature to-
wards computerized analysis of ICP deals with morphological identiﬁcation of individual
ICP waves [8] [31] [46]. An overlooked area is pattern recognition of ICP wave patterns
using machine learning to accompany neurologist and neurosurgical clinics in their ICP
assessment.
Presented in this thesis is a novel approach for describing and performing pattern
recognition on ICP signals and is considered ground research in a poorly documented
ﬁeld.
1
==PAGE:18==
2
CHAPTER 1. INTRODUCTION
1.1
Motivation
The “golden standard” for assessing Intracranial Pressure curves is very limited in regard
to well documented and standardized protocols. At the Clinic of Neurosurgery, Copenha-
gen University Hospital, procedure for ICP assessment in daily practice is1:
• Data is collected through a small surgical invasive intervention where the ICP is
recorded over several hours to days.
• The recorded ICP data is manually transferred to a computer in the clinic where a
provided software platform is used to visualize the ICP curve.
• The software platform only shows the ICP curve with no descriptive statistical func-
tions except for a speciﬁc pressure value to a given time.
• ICP curves are exclusively assessed by visual inspection.
• In diﬃcult cases, the ICP data is exported to the statistical software “R”, for further
analysis.
The interpretation of ICP curves is therefore subjective to each neurologists and will nat-
urally introduce great variation in their assessments. The variation will aﬀect patient
treatment and diagnosis, which could be fatal if done wrong or on an inadequate amount
of data. Of primary interest are A- and B- pressure waves that originate from patholo-
gical alternations and are used in patient diagnosis. While A-waves require intervention,
B-waves have various sub-types where not all are clinical or pathologically signiﬁcant and
therefore, prone to misinterpretation [18]. Furthermore, long-term monitoring in hospitals
and home monitoring are more common and causes a huge increase of data [3]. Manu-
ally examining all data is a slow and time consuming process and in practice, not possible.
An automated system can provide an objective and more consistent classiﬁcation of dia-
gnostic relevant pressure waves in ICP curves.
This will be beneﬁcial for the health
care professional since large amounts of data can be analyzed faster and with less inter-
neurologistic variation. Lastly, a classiﬁcation system could contribute to ﬁnding import-
ant relationships between ICP patterns and physiologic conditions in various diseases.
This leaves a need for the following problem to be solved.
1.2
Problem Statement
There is a need for a tool to automatically detect and classify diagnostic relevant ICP
patterns with equivalent precision to clinical practice to aid in patient assessment and
diagnosis. Moreover, better documentation of ICP patterns is needed to improve consist-
ency.
1.2.1
Speciﬁcation
The objectives of this thesis are multiple and includes developing a data acquisition soft-
ware tool allowing manual annotation of ICP curves. Propose a classiﬁcation protocol
aimed to improve ICP pattern assessment. Apply advanced machine learning on acquired
annotated data to automatically analyze ICP signals for pattern recognition. This can be
summarized in the following:
1Information provided by co-supervisor at the Clinic of Neurosurgery, Copenhagen University Hospital.
==PAGE:19==
1.3. THESIS OVERVIEW
3
• Develop a software platform intended for visual inspection and manual annotation
of ICP signals.
• Compose a classiﬁcation protocol and description for unique ICP wave patterns.
• Use advanced machine learning on annotated ICP signals to promote an automated
ICP pattern recognition algorithm.
1.3
Thesis Overview
The thesis is divided into two main parts. It is important to note that the pagination
does not reﬂect the chronological order the task has been completed in, but mainly to
ease reading and emphasize the solution to the objectives of this project.
Part 1:
Concerning the composition of classiﬁcation protocol, gathering of annotated
ICP patterns and software development with the Clinic of Neurosurgery.
Part 2:
Regards data analysis, signal processing and machine learning on the acquired
ICP data in Part I succeeded by performance evaluation of classiﬁers and
neurologists.
==PAGE:20==
==PAGE:21==
Chapter 2
State of The Art
The purpose of this section is to search the literature for work addressing the problem
statement described in section 1.1. The literature review should cover latest and common
knowledge and generate an overview regarding automatic classiﬁcation of ICP pressure
waves. The literature review will focus on two main areas,
1. the clinical aspects such as ICP pressure wave deﬁnitions and methods on how to
acquire ICP data.
2. The technical aspect concerns feature extraction, signal processing, ﬁltering and
classiﬁcation methods of ICP pressure waves.
The literature search includes articles of less technical character in order to get a better
understanding on when, why and how ICP is used and monitored before being able to
clearly deﬁne and choose appropriate features for the later detection and classiﬁcation
problem. Of equal importance are the understanding of other biological factors or ma-
chinery that could introduce artifacts or noise to the ICP signal. Each article or report will
be summarized by a purpose, a short data description, method, results and conclusion.
Likewise are relevant images and illustrations that will ease the understanding included.
At the end of each article are immediate personal comments on relevance and important
end-points provided. Finally everything is summarized and discussed to achieve a better
overview. The found articles and reports contains these overall subjects:
Clinical
• Lundbergs deﬁnition of A-, B- and C- ICP pressure waves.
• Yokotas deﬁnition of B-wave sub types.
• Home versus in-hospital monitoring of ICP.
• Electrostatic discharge and eﬀect on ICP monitoring.
Technical
• Detection of B-waves.
• Filter eﬀect and comparison on ICP signals.
• Morphological feature extraction of ICP.
• Comparison on two ICP computer based computations strategies.
• Spectral analysis of ICP.

==PAGE:22==
6
CHAPTER 2. STATE OF THE ART
2.1
Clinical Review
Continuous Recording and Control of Ventricular Fluid Pressure in
Neurosurgical Practice.
N. Lundberg,
Acta psychiatrica Scandinavica. Supplementum, 1960, Vol. 36, pp 1-193.
Purpose:
1) Investigate the possibility of continuous long term ICP monitoring while maintaining
pressure control; 2) Investigate the clinical signiﬁcance of ventricular ﬂuid pressure mon-
itoring and its variations in neurosurgical patients.
Data details:
A total of 151 recording varying from < 24 hours to 4 - 8 weeks were obtained on 143
patients. 17 recordings were terminated because of complications leaving a total 134 func-
tioning recordings.
Methods:
Ventricular puncture was used to measure ICP written out on ink-writing potentiometer.
ICP was maintained by a three wave valve to adjust the amount of CSF. Patient ICP
recordings along with history was used to evaluate the clinical ﬁndings. Reference level
was set to the uppermost part if the subarachoidal space to ease the adjustment on the
transducers in daily routine.
Results:
Long-term monitoring is possible with few to no complications. Deﬁnition of A-, B- and
C-waves described as:
A-waves: Plateau like waves in the range of 50 - 100 mmHg of varying duration ranging
from 5 - 20 min. Usually with onset in the wakeful stages and in accordance til
mental or muscular activity.
B-waves: Regular rhythmic oscillations at a rate of 1
2 - 2 per minute with intensity of
varying amplitude in the range of 0 - 50 mmHg. Usually occuring in relation
with depression of wakefulness, both pathological and during normal sleep.
C-waves: Rhythmic oscillation of relatively small amplitude and occurs at rate of 4 - 8
per minute. The intensity ranges from discernibility to 20 mmHg.
Most common clinical symptoms associated with the lower intensity pressure waves (typ-
ically B-waves) were observed as: headache, itching of nose, ﬁxed eyes, among others. In
more high pressure aspects are vomiting, clonic movements of arms and face, trance-like
state ending in coma (typically A-waves) the most common.
Conclusion:
It is possible to continuously record ICP and still control the ventricular ﬂuid pressure.
Furthermore was characteristic pressure wave identiﬁed and described along with patho-
logical ﬁndings on patients.
==PAGE:23==
2.1. CLINICAL REVIEW
7
N. Lundberg was the pioneer in continuously monitoring ICP which is common clinical
routines in modern hospitals. His deﬁnition of A-, B- and C-waves are still used in daily
practice and literature and is essential in the classiﬁcation protocol.

Quality and Safety of Home ICP Monitoring Compared With
In-hospital Monitoring
Andresen Morten, Juhler Marianne, Munch Tina Nørgaard Clinic of Neurosurgery, Copen-
hagen University Hospital, Copenhagen, Denmark.Hydrocephalus, Acta Neurochirurgica
Supplementum, 2012, Vol. 113, pp. 187-191
Purpose:
Evaluate the quality of technical and clinical data obtained by home ICP monitoring op-
posed to in-hospital monitoring.
Data details:
44 Patients had ICP monitored for a mean of 38.4h. The patients where divided into two
groups, one group where home monitored and the second at the hospital.
Methods:
The two groups where statistically (U-test) compared to search for diﬀerence in length,
==PAGE:25==
2.1. CLINICAL REVIEW
9
interruptions, percentage of valid and invalid measurements. Quality of data was measured
on how many clinical selected parameters could be observed in the two groups.
The
parameters are: mean ICP, % of curve with B-waves, No. of episodes with raised ICP
and duration of longest episode with raised ICP. The method is schematically illustrated
in ﬁgure 2.2
Figure 2.2: Overview of home versus in-hospital ICP monitoring.
Illustrated are the
measured parameters along with the statistical output.
Results:
No diﬀerence was found in technical curve quality by comparing number of interruptions
(p = 0.22), percentage of measurement duration with valid curve (p = 0.57), or the ability
to perform adequate clinical evaluation of the data (p = 0.52).
Conclusion:
It is proposed that home monitoring is a feasible and safe alternative to in-hospital with
no signiﬁcant diﬀerence in technical or clinical data quality.
Knowing the diﬀerent methods on how ICP data is acquired and where the limitations
are is useful when developing a system that must be an integrated part of daily routine.
With home monitoring opening the doors to a closer patient follow-up and a huge increase
in the amount of data only emphasizes the need for automatic ICP analysis systems.
Electrostatic Discharges and Their Eﬀect on The Validity of Registered
Values in Intracranial Pressure Monitors
Morten Andresen, M.D., Marianne Juhler, M.D., D.M.Sc. and Ole Cornelius Thomsen,
B.Sc.E.E.
Clinic of Neurosurgery, Copenhagen University Hospital, Copenhagen; and Department of
Electrical Engineering, Technical University of Denmark, Lyngby, Denmark. Journal of
neurosurgery, 2013, Vol. 119, pp 1119-24
Purpose:
To answer 1) are the ICP monitors from major manufacturers aﬀected by Electrostatic
discharges(ESD); 2) are the changes comparable in size; 3) are the changes permanent or
transient in nature; and ﬁnally 4) are the changes modiﬁed.
Data details:
A patient phantom consisted of two 80x60x20 cm containers, ﬁlled with isotonic saline
and connected with a soaked towel. The total volume of water was 40l. Five commercially
available ICP monitors where used to gather a total of 28 individual recordings.
Methods:
The method is schematically illustrated in ﬁgure 2.3 where ﬁve monitors are tested on
==PAGE:26==
10
CHAPTER 2. STATE OF THE ART
diﬀerent clinically relevant “challenges” in order to assess the monitor performance. The
ﬁve ICP monitors are: 1) ﬂuid-ﬁlled transducer (Edwards TruWave); 2) the Codman
MicroSensor; 3) Raumedic Neurovent-P (cable monitor); 4) Raumedic Neurovent-P-tel
(telemetric monitor); and 5) Integra Camino MPM-1. The “challenges” consisted of elec-
trical powered pressure-relieving mattress, ECG cables, pulse oximetry monitor and ﬁnally
an infusion pump. An item was added to run simultaneously in order to increase the “chal-
lenge” level. The monitors were subjected to discharges of 0, ±2, ±4 and±8 kV . A simple
electrostatic voltmeter was used to measures surface voltages and to make continuous re-
cordings.
Figure 2.3: Schematic illustration of ESD eﬀect on commercial available ICP monitors.
Results:
Of the ﬁve diﬀerent ICP monitors only the ﬂuid-ﬁlled transducer and the Camino monitor
were unaﬀected by ESD because they did not contain electrical circuits at the tip of the
transducer. Those with electrical circuits at the tip showed quick recovery to baseline after
experiencing ESD. Only the Codman MicroSensor displayed diﬀerences in the starting and
ending baseline after being challenged (only at ±8kV ). The two Raumedic Neurovent-P-
tel products where very susceptible to presented ESD levels.
Conclusion:
Monitors without an electrical circuitry at the tip of the transducer were all unaﬀected by
ESDs. Discharges at low voltages caused more pronounced but transient signal changes in
the Raumedic monitors. Permanent ICP changes occurred only for the Codman pressure
monitor.
This study tells that ICP recordings are very resilient to other electrical equipment
such as ECG monitoring which is very likely to be present in a neurosurgery operation.
Artifact occurring from ESD will have short spike like character with high intensities and
will easily be spotted by a neurologist and also quite easily removed by a pre-processing
algorithm .

Detection of The B waves in The Oscillation of Intracranial Pressure by
Fast Fourier Transform.
Keita Hara, Susumu Nakatani, Kohji Ozaki, Takuya Ikeda and Heitarou Mogami
SHARP Corporation, Information Systems Group, Nara, Japan; Department of Neurosur-
gery and Central Operating Room Department, Osaka University Hospital, Fukushima-ku,
Osaka City, Japan. MED. INFORM. (1990), VOL. 15, NO. 2, pp. 125-131
Purpose:
Use FFT to automatically detect B-waves from ICP recordings
Data details:
ICP was measured in patients with a normal pressure hydrocephalus from the lumbar
subarachnoid space. The ICP data were sampled at 1,
1
4,
1
8,
1
16 Hz.
Methods:
Fast Fourier Transform is used to analyze the ICP data. Window functions such as Ham-
ming, Hanning, Blackman and cosine are used to reduce sample errors. Furthermore was
a Low Pass ﬁlter used to reduce high noise and a peak-to-peak mean value of 4 s was used
to mask cardiac and respiration induced components. The method is schematically shown
in ﬁgure 2.4.
Figure 2.4: Schematic overview explaining the method for B-wave detection.
Results:
• The FFT analysis showed peaks above 0 dB in the range of 30 – 120 s. Which is
within the identiﬁed range of B-waves described by Lundberg.
• The window functions did not show any eﬀect on the B-wave detection.
• Masking of cardiac and respiration components generated a better B-wave detection.
Conclusion:
B-waves was successfully detected between 30 and 120s using FFT. Window functioning
showed no impact on B-wave detection. Masking of cardiac and respiration induced com-
ponents gives better detection.
This study shows a simple way to recognize and detect B-waves. The detection can
be done on large segments and in smaller windows since this showed no eﬀect on the
detection performance. Masking by ﬁltering the heart rate could help to get a better and
more robust detection performance.

Two Computerized Methods Used to Analyze Intracranial Pressure B
waves: Comparison With Traditional Visual Interpretation
A. Eklund, A. Å. Wilsson, N. Anderson, A. T. Bergenheim, L. D. Koskinen and J. Malm
Departments of Biomedical Engineering and Informatics, and Clinical Neuroscience, Uni-
versity of Umeå, Sweden. Journal of Neurosurgery, 2001, Vol. 94, issue 3, pp. 392-396
Purpose:
Develop a method to estimate the B-wave content in an ICP recording by using computer
algorithms, and to validate these procedures by comparison with the traditional visual
interpretation.
Data details:
ICP was continuously recorded in 8 bed laying patients with IAHS for approx. 17.5–20.8
hours. The ICP was sampled at Fs = 250 Hz by Codman, Raynham,MA ICP monitoring
device.
Methods:
Two methods was implemented where the percent of B-waves (B%) found is used to
validate the two methods.
Method I: The ICP signal (ﬁgure 2.5a) is ﬁltered by band-pass Butterworth ﬁlter (5th
order) with cutoﬀfrequencies corresponding to a periodicity of 0.5 and 2
minutes.
Two threshold (PI) values are used to detect B-wave presences.
B%I was calculated as:
B%I =
P time ICP Amp > ±PI
total measurement time
The method is depicted in ﬁgure 2.5b with threshold PI as the horizontal lines.
Method II: The RMS was computed on the same ﬁltered ICP signal.
The RMS was
calculated for 60s at the time at then thresholded at 1 mmHg (PII). B%II is
computed as:
B%II =
P timeintervalRMS > PII
total measurement time
The method is depicted in ﬁgure 2.5c with threshold PII as the horizontal
line.
The B%I and B%II values were determined over the full monitoring time and for 1, 2, 5,
and 10 hours. For each patient at each monitoring time, these estimations were repeated
20 times at randomly chosen starting points within the total monitoring curve. The mean
values and SDs of B%I and B%II were calculated for each monitoring time.
Results:
The results are best explained by looking at ﬁgure 2.6 where the two methods are compared
with accuracy is percentage and variation from the manual annotated B-waves. As it is
seen in ﬁgure 2.6a for low PI and PII the detection is very sensitive and vice versa for high
thresholds. The variation (ﬁgure 2.6b)of B% between patients was low for both low and
high P values, where middle values yields higher variation. Figure 2.6c shows a decrease
in B% variation as function of increase in monitoring time. The found B% were correlated
with the manually annotated B-waves and found the highest correlation to be r = 0.82

13

I: The bandpass ﬁltered ICP signal with threshold (PI) at amplitude ±1 mmHg and c)

Conclusion:
The two methods perform approximately equal. Both had decreasing variance with in-
crease of monitoring time However method I showed a slightly better mean correlation
with the manual annotation and lower variation in all monitoring times.
Classic Lundberg B-waves can be detected with simple signal processing and shows a

Analysis of Pressure Recordings: Comparison of PCA and Signal
Averaging Based Filtering Methods and Signal Period Estimation
A. Calisto, M. Galeano, A. Bramanti, F. Angileri, G. Campobello, S. Serrano, B. Azzer-
boni Department of Matter Physics and Electronic Engineering, University of Messina,
Italy; and Neurosurgical Department of University Hospital of Messina, Italy.
IEEE
EMBS Buenos Aires, Argentina, 2010, Vol. 2010, pp. 3638-41
Purpose:
Investigate most common ﬁltering techniques and compare the diﬀerent ﬁltering ap-
proaches results by automatic (after ﬁltering) and manual ICP marking.
Data details:
Lumbar ICP was recorded on 16 patients with Normal Pressure Hydrocephalus (NPH)
undergoing an infusion test.
The ICP was recorded on a Codman MicroSensor with
==PAGE:30==
14
CHAPTER 2. STATE OF THE ART
Figure 2.6: Results from Eklund et.
al. [20] (a) showing the accuracy as function of
threshold value. (b) is the variation of B% between patients. (c) shows the variation of
B% as a function of monitoring time.
Fs = 128 Hz.
Methods:
To evaluate the ﬁltering techniques a ICP period estimation algorithm is implemented to
ﬁnd peak and minima. The ICP is depended on the heart rate giving a natural biological
window of 0.8 - 3 Hz. The autocorrelation function is computed on the signal showing
the most dominant periods. Since heart rate variability (HRV) is present a maximum
search is conducted on each period in a window of the assumed max of ±0.15s. Three
diﬀerent ﬁltering techniques are implemented. A) Pre-Filtering, B) Signal averaging and
C) Principal Component Analysis (PCA) ﬁltering.
A) The ICP signal is being ﬁltered by a lowpass ﬁlter with Fpass = 10 Hz, Fstop =
15 Hz Apass = 1 and Astop = 50.
B) The ICP is average ﬁltered after performing the pre-ﬁltering. The average is applied
on various window sizes.
C) The PCA ﬁltering ﬁnds the components that contributes mostly to the signal content
without loosing information. This is done by computing the covariance of the ICP
signal (Y) and then ﬁnd the orthegonal matrix of the eigenvectors (E). The PCA is
then computed as: P = ET YT . Three diﬀerent criteria was set to create diﬀerent
ﬁlters:
PCA1: Cumulative Percent Variance (CPV) is adding the amount of information the
principal components (PC) captures until 90% of the total information is reached
and the descard the rest.
==PAGE:31==
2.2. TECHNICAL REVIEW
15
PCA2: Average Eigenvalue only choosing the amount of PC that captures more than
average. The average is computed as the average eigenvalue.
PCA3: Screenplot method computes the amplitude diﬀerence of consecutive eigenvalue,
i.e. if the computed diﬀerence is below the 10% of the mean value then the last
analyzed eigenvalue and all the following ones are discarded.
The three ﬁltering techniques are then compared based on MSE, Sensitivity (S) and
Positive Predictivity (P). The MSE is compared to window size and Sensitivity and
Positive Predictivity is compute as: S =
n
n+f(−) and P =
n
n+f(+), where n is number
of correct detection of minima before each ICP pulse and f(+), f(−) is number of
false positive and false negative respectively. The method is schematically depicted
in ﬁgure 2.7.
Figure 2.7: Schematic illustration of method for comparing diﬀerent relevant ﬁlters.
Results:
The result from the three diﬀerent ﬁlters are summarized in table 2.2.
Table 2.2: Summary of the diﬀerent ﬁltering techniques and their performance measured
in MSE, Sensitivity and positive prdictivity.

Conclusion:
Based on the results the best ﬁlter through sliding windows are the FIR + average. This
provides the lowest MSE with a high Sensitivity and predictivity. Moreover does the FIR
+ PCA indicate a better performance when whole features and extraction are wanted.
This article provides important information on relevant ﬁlter performances when con-
ducting feature extraction on ICP signals.
This will helps when choosing appropriate
ﬁlters and parameters before performing feature extraction.
Morphological Clustering and Analysis of Continuous Intracranial
Pressure.
Xiao Hu, Peng Xu, Fabien Scalzo, Paul Vespa, Marvin Bergsneider Neural Systems and
Dynamics Laboratory, Department of Neurosurgery, University of California, Los Angeles,
CA 90024 USA; and Neurocritical Care Program, Department of Neurosurgery, University
==PAGE:32==
16
CHAPTER 2. STATE OF THE ART
of California, Los Angeles, CA 90095 USA. IEEE TRANSACTIONS ON BIOMEDICAL
ENGINEERING, 2009, VOL. 56, NO. 3, pp 696-705
Purpose:
Develop a robust algorithm to detect individual nonartifactual ICP-subpeaks and classify
them accordingly.
Data details
153 signal segments of a approx. 5h was randomly selected from continuous ICP and ECG
recordings of 66 patients giving a total of 765h data. Two diﬀerent systems was used to
monitor the ICP and ECG signals. The majority was sampled at 400 Hz the rest at 240 Hz.
Method:
The ICP pulse is depicted in ﬁgure 2.8 where three dominant peaks are identiﬁed and
marked.
Figure 2.8: Representation of two possible types of ICP pulses and the positions of P1,
P2, and P3.
The algorithm is build on multiple steps as is schematically illustrated in ﬁgure 2.9.
Each step includes the following:
ICP pulse detection:
The signal is band-pass ﬁltered with cutoﬀ0.45 to 0.55 times mean HR. The peak of each
individual ICP pulse are found by ﬁnding the R-wave in the ECG. Add a time window
to either side of the R location in the ICP signal. Find the maximum value and location.
Onset of ICP is found by: Find the steepest tangent and make it a line (A). Make a
horizontal line (B) at the minimum prior to the line A. The intersection of A and B is the
onset of the ICP pulse.
Pulse clustering:
A ICP pulse representative is extracted by ﬁrst hierarchical cluster the ICP pulses from
each segment based on their morphological distance. From the largest cluster is an average
ICP pulse (dominant pulse) generated. The Euclidean distance is used as dissimilarity
between the average ICP pulse and presented pulse.
Nonartifactual ICP pulse:
Since the dominant pulse could be aﬀected by noise or artifacts it is further investigated
by the correlation of a validated template pulse. The correlation coeﬃcient determines
whether the dominant pulse is artifactual or not. Pulses that is to artifactual is dismissed.
This is done for all clusters.
Detection of ICP peaks:
The three dominant peaks (P1, P2, P3) are found based on the second derivative (x(n)′′)
==PAGE:33==
2.2. TECHNICAL REVIEW
17
of the ICP pulse and the curvature in each concave region calculated by:
|κ(n)| =
|x(n)′′|
(1 + x(n)′2)
3
2
and by combining a line between the beginning and the end of a concave region. The peak
is located where the distance perpendicular to the line and the data point is maximal.
Optimal assignment:
The found peak candidates a1, a2, ..., aN is then evaluated on a probability density function
(normal) of assigning a candidate ai to Pi. The optimal assignment is acheived by the
maximum of the objective function:
J(i, j, k) = P1(ai) + P2(aj) + P3(ak)
Figure 2.9: Illustration of implemented algorithm.
Results:
The algorithm showed an accuracy of 97.84% in recognizing nonartifactual ICP pulses.
90.17%, 87.56% and 86.53% of the three dominant subpeaks, P1, P2 and P3 respectively
was correctly assigned.
Conclusion:
The presented algorithm proves to be a reliable and robust tool to extract useful morpho-
logical features from ICP signals.
The extrapolation of morphological features from the ICP signal is useful when un-
derstanding the ICP dynamics and could provide useful features when classifying the sub
types of B-waves. The individual pulses are like ECG correlated with pathological changes
and could be a future implementation for a diagnostic system but is outside the scope of
this report.
A Computer-Based Method for Comparisons of Continuous Intracranial
Pressure Recordings Within Individual Cases.
P. K. Eide and A. D. Fremming
Department of Neurosurgery, The National Hospital, University of Oslo; and Sensomet-
rics AS, Oslo, Norway. Acta neurochirurgica, 2003, Vol. 145, pp. 351-358
Purpose:
Comparing two strategies of assessing ICP recordings within individual cases. Either by
calculating the diﬀerence in mean ICP or the diﬀerence in number of ICP elevations.
Data details:
Continuous ICP was measured before and after calvarial expansion surgery in 15 children.
==PAGE:34==
18
CHAPTER 2. STATE OF THE ART
The mean recording time before and after surgery were 7.5h and 9.3h respectively. ICP
was sampled at a rate of 1
5Hz
Methods:
The mean ICP and the number of elevated ICP was calculated in a standard period of
10 hours. The mean ICP is put into three intervals normal (≤10mmHg), borderline
(10mmHg < meanICP < 15mmHg) and abnormal (≥15mmHg). Five ICP elevation
intervals where chosen to be: 20, 25, 30, 35 and 40 mmHg along with the duration time
of each (0.5, 1, 5 and 10 min). Both ICP methods were standardized to 10 hours since
the recordings were rarely 10 hours. Statistical test included Wilcoxon signed rank test,
two-tailed p values and binomial testing.
Figure 2.10: Schematic illustration of computer based evaluation on ICP recording within
cases of pre- and post-operative surgery.
Results:
A reduction of mean ICP with a positive outcome before to after surgery was observed
in 3 of 15 cases. Mean ICP went from 14.1 mmHg before to 11.5 mmHg after surgery.
the wilcoxoon signed rank test showed now diﬀerence (p=0.16) in mean ICP before and
after surgery. Number of elevation of 20 mmHg and 25 mmHg lasting 0.5 min (20 mmHg:
p=0.01, 25 mmHg: p=0.002) and 1 min (20 mmHg: p=0.005, 25 mmHg: p=0.002 showed
a signiﬁcant reduction from before surgery to after.
Conclusion:
When evaluating clinical outcome computing the number of elevation proved to be a more
reliable measure than computing the mean ICP. The smaller elevations of 20 mmHg and
25 mmHg lasting 0.5 min showed more susceptibility than higher elevation.
A common way of using and diagnosing patients is to use mean ICP or numbers of
elevations. Mean ICP could be a useful and simple ﬁrst line of the classiﬁcation algorithm
to extract interesting pressure wave candidates but if more details is needed other features
are necessary.
Spectral Snalysis of Intracranial Pressure Signals Recorded During
Infusion Studies in Patients with Hydrocephalus.
M. García, J. Poza, D. Santamarta, D. Abásolo, P. Barrio, R. Hornero
Biomedical Engineering Group, Department T.S.C.I.T., E.T.S. Ingenieros de Telecomu-
nicación, University of Valladolid, Valladolid Spain; Servicio de Neurocirugía Hospital
Universitario de León, León, Spain; Centre for Biomedical Engineering, Department of
Mechanical Engineering Sciences, Faculty of Engineering and Physical Sciences, Univer-
sity of Surrey, Guildford, United Kingdom. Medical engineering & physics, 2013, Vol. 35,
==PAGE:35==
2.2. TECHNICAL REVIEW
19
Issue. 10, 1490-98
Purpose:
Improve spectral characterization of ICP signals in patients suﬀering from hydrocephalus
and assess the potential approach in the clinical practice.
Data details:
The ICP signal is acquired in the lumbar position from an infusion test on 77 patients
with hydrocephalus. The signal was sampled at 100 Hz and ﬁltered with a FIR BP with
cutoﬀfreq. of 0.1 and 10 Hz.
Methods:
The infusion rate (IR) was 1.5 ml/min until plateau (Pp). ICP recording continued after
the plateau was reached and the infusion stopped. Artifact-free epochs were found by a
neurosurgeon for spectral analysis. The epochs is:
Epoch 0: The basal phase of the infusion test. P0 was determined in this stage.
Epoch 1: Representative of the early infusion phase and usually describes an ascending
slope.
Epoch 2: Corresponds to the plateau phase. Pp was obtained in this stage.
Epoch 3: Represents the recovery phase, once the infusion has stopped and the ICP signal
returns slowly to levels of the basal state.
The signal was divided into windows of 5 s with an overlap of 4 s, where Fourier transform
(FT) and power spectral density (PSD) was conducted on each window. The median freq.
(MF) was found as half of the PSD and the relative power (RP) was computed in three
frequency bands. The complete frequency range was 0.1 to 10 Hz with the three freq
bands at: 0.1 < B1 ≤1Hz,
1 < B2 ≤2Hz,
2 < B3 ≤3Hz. The RP was found in
each window of each frequency band by: RP =
(window of Bi)2
(complete freq range)2 .
Figure 2.11: Schematic illustration of the spectral ICP analysis with statistical results. of
the RP and MF for the diﬀerent epochs.
Results:
Statistical signiﬁcant diﬀerence was found between the MF and the epochs stages. The
lowest and highest MF values were found at the basal line (MF0) and plateau (MF2)
respectively of the ICP signal. For the RP there was only found signiﬁcant diﬀerence
between RP1 and RP2. Here RP1 had highest values at basal line (epoch 0) and decreas-
ing until plateau (epoch 2). The opposite goes for RP2. No clear clinical tendencies were
found when comparing MF and RP to patient data.
==PAGE:36==
20
CHAPTER 2. STATE OF THE ART
Conclusion:
Spectral analysis and characterization of ICP in an infusion test can be used to better
understand CSF dynamics of hydrocephalus. With a signiﬁcant decrease of MF-values
when introducing a more stressful pressure environment. Frequency bands 0.1 - 1 Hz and
1 - 2 Hz showed reciprocal eﬀects when going from basal line to plateau.
This shows that frequency bands do change in accordance to change in ICP. The infu-
sion test forces a plateau like pressure wave which is comparable to the A-waves described
by Lundberg. This could be used to chose relevant features in the detecting and classiﬁc-
ation process.
2.3
Summary
Based on the performed literature review in presented state of the art section is it clear
that little has been done to address the described problem statement in section 1.1. This
emphasizes the motivation for developing a system that can provide an automatic clas-
siﬁcation of ICP pressure waves. Also to start a new research branch and gather more
knowledge in this ﬁeld.
Simple computerized methods have been used to assess ICP data. P. K. Eide [18]
evaluated patient status based on mean ICP and number of elevated ICP over diﬀerent
levels. The study showed that mean ICP is not a preferable feature to base patient status
on, arguing that number of elevated ICP values is of more value. This could be improved
by classifying the elevated ICP pulses hence the imminent problem statement. The found
attempts on automatically detecting A, B, or C waves is done by Hara et. al. [28] and
Eklund et.
al. [20].
In the study performed by Hara, B-waves were simply detected
by taking the FFT of the signal and then thresholded at 0 dB in the power spectrum.
This showed to be an easy way of detecting some B-wave candidates. Eklund only used
threshold and RMS on band-pass ﬁltered ICP signals. Both studies reported promising
detection rate and somewhat robustness.
Related to the FFT did García et. al. [25] analyze the ICP signal by taking the FFT and
PSD in sub frequency bands on diﬀerent epochs on an infusion test. These ICP curves
looks similar to an A-wave showing signiﬁcant changes when moving from baseline ICP to
an increased plateau. From these studies relevant features and their range can be derived.
Besides that, have computerized analysis of ICP mainly been focusing on extracting
morphological features to ﬁnd dominant peaks in single ICP pulse waves. Since the pulse
wave itself in terms of amplitude and placement of dominant peaks can provide information
on the dynamics of the CSF. This is naturally an important area but doesn’t quite solve
the problem at hand. However these studies showed promising potential when extracting
features from the ICP curves and could be useful when making the system more robust.
Lastly did Calisto et. al. [7] experiment with the most common ﬁlters in the ICP
area and performed a assessment based on location of ICP pulse maxima and minima al-
gorithm. The results showed that a FIR LP ﬁlter followed by a moving average performed
best when windowing the ICP signal, while FIR LP and a PCA ﬁlter could provide better
results when extracting features on larger segments. This is relevant when deciding on
which ﬁlters to use and could save time even though the FIR LP and averaging ﬁlters are
==PAGE:37==
2.3. SUMMARY
21
a common choice and a very known ﬁlter type.
Clinical studies showed how ICP is monitored, deﬁned in terms of pressure range,
duration, oscillation, shape and clinical correlation to patient condition. Most important
are the pressure wave deﬁnition made by Lundberg [36] where A, B, and C-waves is
described and depicted.
Later followed further subdivision of B-waves by Yokota et.
al. [52] and will be an essential part when describing the ICP patterns in classiﬁcation
protocol. Furthermore are diﬀerent commercialized ICP monitoring devices evaluated by
Andresen et. al. [4] investigating the inﬂuence of ESD and evaluated the consequences
hereof. The results showed that only one device had a permanent baseline change, which
obviously could be fatal if interpreted wrongly. Same author showed that home monitoring
is equally safe and reliable as in-hospital leading to an increase in patient follow-up and
data amount [3].

Chapter 3
Clinical Background
There are many diseases leading to an increase in ICP [28] [46]. The pathophysiology
and etiology for some of the diseases are known and can be treated while, others are still
unknown and treatments are focused on reducing symptoms.
In the following chapter ICP, regulatory mechanisms and appearance of ICP in healthy and
unhealthy subjects are explained. Furthermore the most common diseases in the Clinic are
summarized and set in relation to the ICP curves. Lastly ICP monitoring procedure and
clinical products are described providing a complete history of what patients experience
from admission to discharge.
3.1
Intracranial Pressure
The brain is anatomically and functionally the most complex organ in the human body.
It accounts for approx.
2% of the total body weight but is given approx.
1
6 of the
resting cardiac-output and consumes approx. 20% of the body’s oxygen. The brain is
well protected from external forces by the skull and cushioned by the cerebrospinal ﬂuid
(CSF). The intracranial pressure (ICP) is deﬁned as the pressure within the intracranial
cavity and determined by three factors [42]:
1. The pressure-volume relationship between the brain tissue (∼80%), the CSF (∼
10%) and the blood (∼10%).
2. the Monro-Kellie hypothesis and
3. the compliance of the brain and its ability to buﬀer changes in intracranial volume.
The greatest factor on ICP is the CSF, which is produced by the ﬁltration of the blood in
plexus choroideus inside the ventricles in the brain. The CSF is then transported through
the 3rd and 4th ventricle to the subarachnoid space and the spinal cord [45]. Figure 3.1
shows the location of the ventricles and the placement of CSF in the brain.
23
==PAGE:40==
24
CHAPTER 3. CLINICAL BACKGROUND
Figure 3.1: Illustration of ventricle location in the brain.
3.1.1
Pressure-Volume Relationship, Compliance and The
Monro-Kellie Hypothesis
The brain tissue, CSF and blood are compactly placed inside the nonexpandable skull.
These three volume types control the ICP and are normally kept within the range of 0 - 15
mmHg in supine position [36] [42] [2]. Since the three types are practically incompressible,
a change in one will naturally aﬀect the other two. This is done by a reciprocal eﬀect
where an increase of one component will cause an opposite eﬀect from the two others
meaning that pressure will always be constant (k). This is known as the Monro-Kellie
hyposthsis and can be explained by:
VCSF + VBlood + VTissue + VUnknown = k
where VCSF , VBlood and VTissue are the volume of the CSF, blood and brain tissue re-
spectively. VUnknown is an extra component such as brain tumor, hemorrhage or nothing
(in normal situations). Since all volumes are conﬁned within the skull, this adds more
volume and less ﬂexibility for the other three components to alternate the ICP. The ICP
is regulated through diﬀerent compensatory mechanisms:
1. Pressure alternation through displacement:
Since CSF is the most easily displaced component it can, if needed, be displaced from
the ventricles and the subarachnoid space to the spinal cord or spinal subarachnoid
space.
2. Pressure alternation through absorption/production:
If the displacement of the CSF is insuﬃcient, an increase or decrease in the absorp-
tion through the blood system can likewise regulate the ICP to preferred homeo-
stasis. Likewise, an increase or decrease in production of CSF will cause the same
eﬀect.
3. Pressure alternation through compression:
The blood carried in the low pressure venous system can undergo some compression
along with the brain tissue and alternate the pressure.
The ability of a substance to be compressed or displaced is referred to as compliance. The
compliance (C) is deﬁned as the ratio between unit volume (V) and pressure (P) given by
C = ∆V
∆P . The compliance is illustrated in ﬁgure 3.2. The compensatory mechanisms just
described show the ability to keep an almost constant ICP level despite the increase in
volume (A - B). When the compensatory mechanisms reach their limits and fail (B - D),
the ICP increases dramatically as volume increases [42].
==PAGE:41==
3.1. INTRACRANIAL PRESSURE
25
Figure 3.2: Volume-Pressure curve showing the compliance mechanisms of the brain.
Figure inspired from [42].
3.1.2
ICP in Patients
Normal ICP
The ICP is dependent on the rate and force of the heart [8]. As illustrated in ﬁgure 3.3
and as described by [31] [24], the ﬁrst peak (P1) is linked to the systolic phase of the heart
cycle with a small delay reported to be 72.6 ± 19.5ms [31] - also called the arterial pulse
wave. The second peak (P2) is linked to the respiratory part (tidal wave) and the last
peak is due to slower responses called the dicrotic notch or slow wave. The minimum of
the pressure wave is called the diastolic position. Studies indicate that the position and
amplitude of the peaks can relate to the condition of the patient [8]. P2 is believed to
indicate cerebral compliance [9]. In ﬁgure 3.3, two schematic ICP pulses (a and b) are
viewed taken from Calisto et. al. [8] and two ICP pulses from the same patient at diﬀerent
times in provided ICP data(c and d). c) is taken in a stable period as opposed to d) at a
plateau of an A-wave. Clear diﬀerences can be seen between the two ICP pulses with d)
having both higher amplitude and elevated P2.
(c)
(d)
Figure 3.3: (a) and (b) schematic representations of two possible types of ICP pulses and
the positions of P1, P2, and P3. Taken from Calisto et. al. [8]. (c) and (d) are patient
ICP pulses taken from in vivo measurement with three clear dominant peaks
Zooming out from individual ICP pulses and looking at the ICP curve from a macro
perspective, individual pulses are no longer visible but events and patterns are revealed.
==PAGE:42==
26
CHAPTER 3. CLINICAL BACKGROUND
A normal ICP is shown in ﬁgure 3.4. The mean ICP is between 0 - 15 mmHg in supine
position and can drop to -10 to 10 mmHg while standing or walking [2]. These ranges
are stable and will appear in the ICP curve as a rapid increase or decrease and quickly
stabilize. Deviating from this is considered abnormal - however, it is still unknown if some
abnormalities are present in healthy subjects or are indicators of disease [36] [52].
Figure 3.4: Example of ICP within the normal range. Small irregularities can be observed
Abnormal ICP
As explained in the State of The Art section 2 there are 3 main ICP waves: A, B and C,
as described by Lundberg [36]. Further subdivision of the B-waves was done by Yokota
et. al. [52]. These ICP waves are considered abnormal and have their own properties in
shape, intensity and duration as well as occurrence in relation to patient activity level and
condition:
A-waves: Large plateau-like waves emerge in intervals of varying duration (5-20 min)
and intensity (50-100 mmHg).
Usually occurs when patients are awake and
in correlation with motor or emotional activity. Symptoms associated with A-
waves range from headache to more transient symptoms often of paroxysmal
character. Coma and vomiting are common in severe stages of A-waves. The
severity of the symptoms are determined by the intensity. A-waves are reduced
by forced breathing, urea and drainage. Typical A-waves are shown in ﬁgure 3.5
top curve.
B-waves: Regular rhythmic oscillations at a rate of 1
2 - 2 per minute with intensity of
varying amplitude in the range of 0 - 50 mmHg. Persistence for a longer period
of time is mostly associated with cerebral dysfunction. B-waves usually occur
when patients are fully awake and are aﬀected by breathing and can be reduced
by forced or artiﬁcial breathing [52]. B-waves are divided into subtypes and
shown in ﬁgure 3.5 middle curves.
Type I: Clustered B-waves appear episodically and in clusters with an intensity
between 25 – 75 mmHg. All patients are awake or in the REM stage of
sleep.
Type II: Persistent high pressure B-waves appearing continuously for a longer period
of time (up to a whole night) with an intensity range from 40 - 100 mmHg
and at a rate of 1 - 2 minutes.
Type III: Regular B-waves appearing continuously with a rate of 1 - 2 minutes for
60 minutes and at intensities between 10 - 30 mmHg.
==PAGE:43==
3.2. COMMON DISEASES
27
Figure 3.5: The ﬁve diﬀerent pressure waves identiﬁed by Yokota et. al. [52]
C-waves: Rhythmic oscillations of relatively small amplitude that occurs at a rate of 4 -
8 per minute. The intensity ranges from discernibility to 20 mmHg. C-waves
are particularly observed on an A-wave plateau. C-waves have limited relation
to pathological dysfunctions.
3.2
Common Diseases
There are four major conditions dominating the daily practice at the Clinic of neurosur-
gery, Copenhagen University Hospital1 and also found in the literature. The most com-
mon are 1) Hydrocephalus, 2) Idiopathic Intracranial Hypertension, 3) subarachnoid/in-
tracerebral Hemorrhage and 4) Head trauma. All above described ICP pressure waves
are observed over all patient groups and thereby tell more about the patient status than
speciﬁc diagnosis. The two patient groups where ICP monitoring is regularly used are
described from [42].
3.2.1
Hydrocephalus
Deﬁnition and Occurrence
Hydrocephalus is deﬁned as abnormal dilation of the ventricles in the brain caused by an
increase of CSF volume due to disturbances in CSF ﬂow, absorption or secretion. This is
illustrated in ﬁgure 3.6.
Hydrocephalus typically occurs in children within the ﬁrst year of life and in the elderly.
Due to diﬀerence in age of debut, pathophysiology and severity of ICP elevation, hydro-
cephalus is divided into sub-categories:
Age of Debut
1Information provided by co-supervisor Marianne Juhler and Morten Andresen
==PAGE:44==
28
CHAPTER 3. CLINICAL BACKGROUND
Figure 3.6: Illustration of normal (left) and hydrocephalus (right)
• Infantile (from birth to 18 months)
• Juvenile (18 months to 14 years)
• Adult (above 14 years)
Pathophysiology
• Communicating hydrocephalus (absorption defect)
• Non-communicating hydrocephalus (blocking of CSF pathways)
Severity
• High pressure hydrocephalus
• Normal pressure hydrocephalus (NPH) usually secondary to other brain disease or
age
Symptoms and Clinical Observations
Infantile hydrocephalus
In newborns extreme enlargement of the skull is a certain sign of hydrocephalus. The head
is more penetrable by light and will look like an orange balloon. Clear signs of increased
ICP are drowsiness alternating with intense crying, clear failure to thrive, decreased appet-
ite and vertical gaze palsy (Parinaud’s Syndrome). Untreated hydrocephalus will manifest
in diminishing intellectual development, optic atrophy, gait disturbances and spasticity.
Juvenile and Adult hydrocephalus
For the juvenile acute symptoms can appear over a short period of time. The primary
symptoms are headache, vomiting, dizziness and visual disturbances. In severe cases are
impairment of consciousness and attacks of opisthotonus (spasms where the person is
ﬂexed backward). Symptoms advance with increasing ICP. In gradual development of in-
creased ICP, the symptoms less clear and will show in cognitive symptoms and school/be-
havior complications. This is often misinterpreted to be of social origin. In adults hy-
drocephalus is mostly a secondary eﬀect of a head trauma or hemorrhage. Elderly and
==PAGE:45==
3.2. COMMON DISEASES
29
adult cases of Normal Pressure Hydrocephalus (NPH) are most common. The symptoms
are manifested by diﬃculty in walking, impaired cognitive functions and loss of bladder
control. These are called the “NPH-triad”.
Diagnosis
Routine measurements of the circumference of the head in newborns are plotted into
circumference curves that are the normal growth of the head and can be compared. This
will lead to a MRI or CT scan to conﬁrm enlarged ventricles as seen in ﬁgure 3.7. MRI
and CT are the most important tools in Juvenile and Adult hydrocephalus. The imaging
shows the state and the pathophysiology. Measurements of ICP and CSF ﬂow are done
as a supplement in case the symptoms and imaging were unclear. This is important when
diﬀerentiating between NPH and dementia. In patients undergoing long term treatment,
ICP monitoring can help determine the CSF ﬂow.
Figure 3.7: MRI images of ventricle size in normal subjects (A) and in subjects suﬀering
from hydrocephalus (B).
3.2.2
Idiopathic Intracranial Hypertension
Deﬁnition and Occurrence
Idiopathic Intracranial Hypertension (IIH) is a condition with an increased ICP > 15
mmHg for normal weight patients and >18 mmHg for obese. Furthermore, MRI and CT
images are normal. The incident is 1-2/100000 and up to 3/100000 in obese women. In
children there is no diﬀerence between genders, but will unbalance in adulthood to mainly
target women. Especially overweight women between the age of 15-44 have a particularly
high incidence at 20/100000.
Etiology and Pathogenesis
No clear cause IIH of is known2. Among the suggested reasons are endocrine disturb-
ances, head trauma and intracranial infection.
The only coherence is associated with
obesity mainly in women. Men and children suﬀering from IIH are mostly normal weight
indicating a diﬀerence in pathogenesis between the two patient groups. Moreover, the
pathophysiological mechanisms are not fully understood. However studies have shown
that some patients with IIH have increased extra- and intracellular water content in the
brain. Others have an increased resistance in CSF outlet.
2Provided by co-supervisors at Copenhagen University Hospital
==PAGE:46==
30
CHAPTER 3. CLINICAL BACKGROUND
Symptoms and Clinical Observations
The most important symptoms are pulse-related gradual increased headache. Visual dis-
turbances are second most frequent characterized by blurred vision, double vision, episodes
of vision loss or black spots in the vision. Other common symptoms are nausea, pulsing
tinnitus, dizziness and bad short term memory. Long tern, high ICP aﬀects vision and
ends in blindness.
Diagnosis
The diagnosis is based on exclusion of other diseases. CT and MR are used to ensure
no other reasons for the increased ICP. ICP is monitored for 1-2 days to conﬁrm the
diagnosis and is conﬁrmed by an elevated ICP in the range of 18-28 mmHg and the
presence of abnormal ICP pressure waves. Long term treatment by tele-metric is done
when eﬀects of treatment need to be followed.
3.3
ICP Monitoring
There are several commercialized ICP monitoring devices on the market. A single device
often oﬀers multiple functions such as ICP, temperature monitoring or oxygen partial
pressure [14]. Two types are depicted in ﬁgure 3.8 showing a Raumedic-TPO (a) and
Intgra Camino (b) ICP monitor.
The ICP monitor consists of a probe placed in the CSF with an invasive procedure
(a)
(b)
Figure 3.8: Two types of ICP monitors used in the Clinic of Neurosurgery, Copenhagen
University Hospital. a) Raumedic-TPO and b) Integra Camino.
through a small hole in the skull. The probe has a transducer converting the ICP pressure
to an electrical signal. The signal is transported to a monitor that records and displays
relevant values, such as mean ICP, temperature, etc. There are two major types of ICP
monitoring devices:
1. Cable ICP monitor: Sits in the head while monitoring (ﬁgure 3.8) and data is
transferred through a cable to an external monitor where data is stored.
2. Tele-metric ICP monitor: The probe (ﬁgure 3.9a) is permanently placed in the skull
with the transferring part of the probe placed between the skin and the skull. The
skin is then closed and data is transferred wirelessly from the probe to a receiver
(ﬁgure 3.9b) on the other side of the skin.
The receiver is connected to a data
monitor (ﬁgure 3.9c and d) [2] [4].
==PAGE:47==
3.3. ICP MONITORING
31
Figure 3.9: The ICP monitoring equipment with; (a) The Neurovent-P-TEL, Raumedic
AG telemetric probe, (b) the receiver, (c) and (d) the data monitor viewed from the front
and back. Pictures provided by M. Andresen [2]
3.3.1
Placement of ICP Monitoring Device
The placement of the probe is important when measuring the ICP. As described by An-
dresen et al. [2] this is done surgically by a minimally invasive procedure introducing
minimal risk to the patient. The four most documented places are depicted in ﬁgure 3.10.
The “gold standard” was to measure the ICP inside the ventricles (Intraventricular D )
because of the direct access to the ventricles. Later several studies have shown that pla-
cing the probe Intraparenchymal C ) provides the same reliable ICP curve. It is still
unclear if Subdural A and Epidural B locations can be used as a reliable third or fourth
alternative. Studies have shown that these locations generate inaccurate ICP values but
still capture the wave patterns. The choice of placement ( C or D ) is based on need for
drainage and/or the ease of placement of the intraparenchymal C probes.
3.3.2
Monitor Devices
The devices used at the Clinic of Neurosurgery, Cph. Univ. Hospital are three cable
devices (Codman MicroSensor, Integra Camino MPM-1 and Raumedic Neurovent-P) and
one telemetric monitor (Raumedic Neurovent-P-tel). Little technical information is avail-
able online and only user manuals and test documentation are provided to the clinic.
However it is known at the clinic that the cable devices sample data at 100 Hz, whereas
the telemetric only measures at 5 Hz [4]. The Raumedic Neurovent series, which is the
most used at the clinic3, oﬀers a very low baseline drift over 5 days of 0.6 ±0.9 mmHg and
a dynamic range of -40 to +400 mmHg in temperature interval of +25 to +45 C◦[13].

Chapter 4
ICP Registration and Classiﬁcation
System
An earlier DTU bachelor project [17] proved that annotation via a software platform is
possible.
The ICP Registration and Classiﬁcation System (ICP-RCS) is developed in
collaboration with the Clinic of Neurosurgery, CPH, Univ. Hospital. The system allows
the health care professionals to manually annotate ICP curves. The ﬁnal system builds
on several meetings an test runs by the neurologists. This is the ﬁrst attempt to manually
classify ICP events using a software system with associated classiﬁcation protocol.
Each iteration in the development of ICP-RCS includes the following steps:
1. Updated version of the ICP-RCS
2. Test phase
3. Evaluation of ICP-RCS considering work ﬂow, GUI design etc.
4. Discussion of changes, new functions and layout for the next version of the ICP-RC
System
Both stakeholders agreed on the importance of an early start. Meetings were used as basis
for discussing and improving the program. The time between meetings was allocated for
implementation of a new version and testing; this was repeated until the system was good
enough for manual scoring of ICP curves. In the following chapter the ICP-RC System is
described in greater detail. The ICP-RC System is developed in MATLAB.
4.1
ICP Data File
At the Clinic, recorded ICP data from the described monitors are visualized in “Datalog-
ger” - a system provided by Raumedic® in the Clinic. Patient ICP data can be exported
from the Datalogger system to a .txt ﬁle. The txt ﬁle is a space saparated ﬁle with three
variables, “Date”, “Time” and “mmHg” (ICP values). Below is an example of a raw data
ﬁle.
Date is represented as [DD:MM:YYYY], Time as [hh:mm:ss:s] and mmHg is the
measured ICP.

This is not the most eﬃcient way to represent numbers.
As an example
occupies a 12 hour ICP recording 121 MB which requires a lot of storage space since
typical monitoring time are 6 - 48 hours per patient.
4.1.1
Down sampling
As mentioned in the clinical background chapter 3 the ICP are sampled at 5 Hz and 100 Hz
depending on the monitor device. In the clinical practice no dominant choice of sampling
frequency is preferred since this is determined by the product. Information in the signal
can be gained from the frequency composition. Naturally, there is more information at
100 than at 5 Hz. In order to make a robust software system and later machine learning is
the 100 Hz signal down sampled to 5 Hz (factor of M = 20). The downsampling are done
in two steps. 1) Lowpass ﬁltering the signal to avoid aliasing the down sampled signal
and 2) down sample the signal by the factor M to arrived at the desired sampling rate.
When down sampling a signal every M sample is taken out yielding: xnew[m] = xold[M ·n]
which corresponds to compressing the signal in time.
A compression in time yields a
stretch in frequency meaning that the bandwidth of the down sampled signal can overlap
with its higher order replicates and create aliasing. That is why the signal is ﬁrst lowpass
ﬁltered in order to ensure that no aliasing occurs [37]. The down sampling is implemented
using the MATLAB function resample.
4.2
System Overview
To create an overview of the system a use case diagram in ﬁgure 4.1 is provided, to show
the involved actors and basic functions of the system. User is the main actor and interacts
with the system. The user will typically be the neurologists or other experts with a medical
background and experience in assessing ICP curves. The system will interact with the
user through a Graphical User Interface (GUI).
==PAGE:53==
4.3. GRAPHICAL USER INTERFACE
37
Figure 4.1: Use case diagram showing two actors interacting with the system. The user
is the main actor and the system pulls or pushes data to and from a storage unit or data
base.
4.3
Graphical User Interface
The GUI is made by the “guide” (Graphical User Interface Design Environment) in MAT-
LAB allowing for graphical design of interfaces. A mock-up of the ﬁnal GUI is shown in
ﬁgure 4.2. The ICP curve and corresponding spectrogram occupies the majority of the
GUI since visual assessment and annotation of ICP curves is the purpose of the ICP-RC
System. Navigating in the ICP curve is done by sliders for both x and y axis. The class
buttons are placed near the ICP curve to make the annotation easy and fast. Patient info
is static text providing relevant information concerning name of patient, date, length and
time of day of the measurement. The Open, Load, Save and Convert to mat functions are
“hidden” in a dropdown menu as known from many other systems.
Figure 4.2: Schematic illustration of the GUI. Dominating the GUI are the ICP curve
and spectrogram. To the left are the class and erase buttons for annotation. A dropdown
menu will hide the convert, Open, Load and Save functions.

The following section from the overview use case diagram in ﬁgure 4.1 will step-wise

annotations, open and save/load patient ﬁles. Subsequently, the implementation of the
speciﬁc use cases are explained by activity diagrams and code/function calls with relevant
argumentation and explanation of choices and to enhance reproducibility. Note, that only
the three top use cases are provided in the main report. The interested reader is referred
to Appendix C for complete description.
4.4.1
Use Case 1: Convert txt to mat File
Since ICP data only is available in txt-format and the ASCII data representation is a
heavy format to work with for MATLAB causing the computers to run out of memory.
To overcome this problem is the txt ﬁle converted to a less memory heavy format like
MATLAB’s own .mat format. The mat format uses ﬂoating point for numeric values [39].
In the following use case are explained steps essential for converting available txt ﬁles to
mat ﬁles.

4.4.2
Implementation
As seen in the activity diagram (ﬁgure 4.3) are the system steps implemented by fol-
lowing callback from the main ﬁle found in Appendix B.1. The following code gets the
ﬁlename and path from the user, using MATLAB’s uigetfile function and then calls the
ConvertToMat.m script.
function mbsConv_Callback(hObject, eventdata, handles)
% Get patient file and path.
[FileName,PathName,~] = uigetfile({'*.txt';'*.*'}, ...
'Select the ICP file', handles.ADC.dataPath);
% Convert selected file
ConvertToMat
end
The ConverToMat function computes the size of the txt ﬁle by reading number of line,
==PAGE:56==
40
CHAPTER 4. ICP REGISTRATION AND CLASSIFICATION SYSTEM
line by line. This is possible because the txt ﬁle is row based where each row accounts
for a single value entry. Then the number of 3 hour chunks the ﬁle can be split up in,
is calculated. 3 hours are selected due to typical length of measurements and memory
issues, implemented by:
% Read File
fileID
= fopen(filename,'r');
% Initialize file size counter
count
= 0;
fs
= 100;
%[Hz]
% Count file size line by line
while ischar(fgetl(fileID))
count
= count +1;
end
% compute number of chunks
t
= 3;
% [hours]
fileStep
= round(count/(t*fs*3600));
% Split into t hour chunks with fs
The corresponding start and end-line of the current chunk are computed by the fs and
the value of the iterator. Note that the start line is the second line due to the header.
Loading the chunk is done by the loadnPartData found in Appendix B.1. As discussed
is the ﬁle downsampled to 5 Hz. The time stamp, ICP values and date are converted to
the mat-format and saved in a temporary mat-ﬁle.
% Compute start and end location for chunk in file
% start line 2 because of header
startRow
= (i-1)*(t*100*3600)+2;
endRow
= i*(t*100*3600)+1
% Get Date, Time and ICP curve for chunk
[Date, Time, ICP] = loadnPartData(filename, startRow, endRow);
% Resample ICP from 100 Hz down to 5 Hz
ICP
= resample(ICP, 5,100);
ICP
= num2cell(ICP);
Lastly are all the temporary ﬁles concatenated into a single ﬁle and saved at the same
location and name. The txt ﬁle will only have to be converted once.

Implementation
The opening function uses the same uigetfile as in use case 1. The Time, Date and ICP
data are likewise loaded in the same manner using the load call in MATLAB. The data
is put in the main MATLAB struct which is used to send data back and forth through
function calls behind the GUI.
% Put data in ADC struct
handles.ADC.data.xTime
= xTime;
handles.ADC.data.ICP
= ICP;
handles.ADC.data.fs
= fs;
handles.ADC.data.dateStart
= finalData.dateStart;
handles.ADC.data.dateEnd
= finalData.dateEnd;
Plotting the ICP curve and spectrogram is done by two separate functions since the same
needs to be done in other functions and thereby programed as a more object orientated
coding scheme. The plotting function gets the slider values and plots a 42 min window
of the ICP curve. The y axis ranges from [-20 mmHg, 80 mmHg] as this will cover most
==PAGE:59==
4.4. USE CASES
43
of the ICP range for more than 95 % of all patients. These values are acquired with the
neurologists by experimenting with diﬀerent time and amplitude ranges. Auto scaling
was tried but showed to create a great deal of confusion since small wave patterns could
suddenly appear very large if a noise artifact is navigated out of the viewed range. Even
though the doctors can see the amplitude value on the y axis is unchanged, the visual
will impact cause them to score diﬀerently. Same is done for the spectrogram showing the
viewed ICP curve with a window length of 80 samples and 90% overlap. This was chosen
after trying a great variety of window lengths and by visual inspection together with the
neurologists were above mentioned settings picked.
The plotICP function is found in
Appendix B.1 with key lines:
% plot ICP on GUI with time (hours) and ICP (mmHg)
plot(handles.ADC.data.xTime, handles.ADC.data.ICP, 'k');
% set time and amplitude axis
set(handles.axesMain,'XTick', tickLocation);
set(handles.axesMain,'XTickLabel',handles.ADC.data.timeStamps);
% set 0.7*60 min window
axis([0 0.7 -20 80]);
% View labels
xlabel('Time [HH:MM]'), ylabel('amplitude [mmHg]');
% get the view ICP range
xRange = 1:0.7*3600*handles.ADC.data.fs;
axes(handles.axesSpec)
% window size
win = 80;
% [samples]
% Plot spectrogram with 90% overlap, within the view ICP range
spectrogram(handles.ADC.data.ICP(xRange),win, win*0.9,256 ,...
handles.ADC.data.fs ,'yaxis');
The setPatientInfo function displays the name, date, total duration alongside start and
end time of the measurement on the GUI. The information will only change when a new
patient is opened or loaded. Lastly, the buttons to annotate each class are per default
disabled when the program is started. The ﬁnal step for the Open/Load function is to
enable them so manual scoring can be commenced.
% Checks if button is disabled and enables them if true
if strcmp(get(handles.pbAAA, 'Enable'), 'off')
for i = 1:length(handles.ADC.labels)
tempStr = ['pb' handles.ADC.labels{i}];
set(handles.(tempStr), 'Enable', 'on', 'BackgroundColor', ...
handles.ADC.colorType{i});
end
end

4.5. THE FINAL ICP-RC SYSTEM
45
Implementation
There are six diﬀerent class buttons all with a unique name to identify their respective
class. The button labels are “smA”, “AA”, “LAL”, “Reg”, “Irr”, “Sol”. Each class are
assigned a value from 1 - 6 being 1: smA, 2: AA, 3: LAL, 4: Reg, 5: Irr and 6: Sol.
When a class button is pushed the system gets graphical input by the ginputc MATLAB
function that forces the user to provide input to the system. The user annotates the ICP
event by clicking at the beginning and end of the event on the graph. The following code
takes the values from the user input and assigns them to an annotation list in the main
struct (ADC) for the identiﬁed class.
% find the selected label
label
= handles.ADC.labels{s};
% Put coordinates in to selected label and add if values are already
% there.
annCoor = handles.ADC.annotation.(label);
annCoor = [annCoor; annX, annY];
% Put into handles at the selected label and write out
handles.ADC.annotation.(label) = annCoor;
Annotated values are then plotted by the plotAnnotation function that iterates through
all classes and highlights the area using vfill for each coordinate pair (xstart, xend). Each
class has diﬀerent colors reﬂecting the assumed pathological severity of the speciﬁc class
as seen in the classiﬁcation protocol. The color choice follows the commonly used red,
yellow and green with green having no/little pathological consequences and red being very
pathological. Blue is used to indicate normal ICP (Solid).
% get all start and end values for each class
xStart
= handles.ADC.annotation{:,1};
xEnd
= handles.ADC.annotation{:,2};
% get annotation labels
annLabel
= handles.ADC.annotation{:,3};
% iterate through all classes
for i = 1:length(xStart)
% idetify class
idx
= strcmp(handles.ADC.labels, annLabel(i,:));
[c, p]
= find(idx == 1);
% fill area with class specific color to mark annotation
vfill([xStart(i) xEnd(i)], handles.ADC.colorType{p},'edgecolor','none',
'facealpha',.3);
end
4.5
The Final ICP-RC System
The ﬁnal version of the ICP-RCS is shown in ﬁgure 4.7a and b where the six classes from
the protocol are represented by a button. The colors of the buttons represent the patho-
logical severity of the class. Blue is represented as “normal” and then going from green
as little pathological →“normal” to yellow over in red indicating increase in pathological
severeness. The ranges on the x axis (time) is determined by trying diﬀerent ranges to-
gether with the neurologists agreeing on showing a time interval of 42 min (0.7 hours).
This gave the doctors a satisfying range to identify events and trends in the ICP signal.
