4.6
Spectrogram
The spectrogram is added because it provides frequency information about the ICP signal
to the neurologists, which can be very hard to identify only by analyzing the ICP signal
in time domain. This is a completely new way to assess ICP signals for the Clinic and is
not attempted before as an additional measure for ICP classiﬁcation and could ultimately
provide a better and more robust description of each class. Naturally, analyzing the spec-
trogram will have to be practiced and explained before the neurologist completely can
beneﬁt from the extra information achieved the spectrogram. In the following section the
spectrogram will be explained in greater detail. For the Solid, Irregular, Regular B and
A-wave classes corresponding spectrograms are exempliﬁed and observations described.
The spectrogram is computed on 80 sample windows with 90% overlap. This window
length gave the suﬃcient trade-oﬀbetween time/frequency resolution.
4.6.1
Spectrogram for Solid Wave Pattern
The spectrogram and ICP curve for the Solid wave pattern are shown in ﬁgure 4.8 within
the two blue lines. At ﬁrst glance three clear dominant bands around 0 (DC), approx 1
and 2 Hz are visible. As stated in the Clinical Background chapter 3 the ICP is linked to
the heart [31] [24] [8]; hence, the frequency band around 1 Hz (60 beats/min) is identiﬁed
as the Heart rate. The second harmonic is visible as the band at double the frequency of
==PAGE:64==
48
CHAPTER 4. ICP REGISTRATION AND CLASSIFICATION SYSTEM
the Heart rate.
Figure 4.8: Blue lines marking Solid wave pattern showing the analog ICP and spectro-
gram. Spectrogram is very stable with 3 distinct bands around DC, 1 and 2 Hz
4.6.2
Spectrogram for Irregular Wave Pattern
Typical Irregular patterns are shown in ﬁgure 4.9 within the green lines. It is clearly
seen that the frequency content are more spread out or “blurry” over all frequency com-
ponents, as opposite to the solid pattern. Still the heart rate is visible but less clear in
the second harmonic. Observed in the beginning of the ICP curve will small irregularities
be very similar to solid pattern but is better diﬀerentiated by the more active spectrogram.
Figure 4.9: More blurry spetrogram with less destinct bands at DC and 1 Hz, with an
almost invisible 2nd harmonic
4.6.3
Spectrogram for Regular B-wave Pattern
In ﬁgure 4.10 within the two yellow lines are Regular B waves present . The spectrogram
on the contrary to ﬁgure 4.9 are less spread out but has very clear DC, the heart rate band
and its second harmonics. The spectrogram is very similar to the one in ﬁgure 4.8 where
Solid pattern is shown. A diﬀerence can be noticed as a “ghost” band just above DC is
observed. This band however, has a very low amplitude. The beneﬁt of the spectrogram
==PAGE:65==
4.6. SPECTROGRAM
49
is visible at the end of the ICP curve, after the yellow line. Here the ICP curve starts to
be more irregular which can be diﬃcult to see on the ICP curve but is more clear in the
spectrogram with a very sudden unset of “blurring” around the 1 Hz band.
Figure 4.10: Inside yellow lines are Regular B-waves shown. Spectrogram are comparable
to the Solid pattern except extra low frequency band appearing “ghost” like appearance.
4.6.4
Spectrogram for A-wave Pattern
Spectrogram of A-waves are depicted in ﬁgure 4.11. Appears as “clouds” in the spectro-
gram ﬁlling most of the frequency bandwidth, but otherwise have an irregular appearance
and is better recognized on the ICP curve. This also represents the rare class and doesn’t
occur to often.
Figure 4.11: Classic A waves mostly visible on the analog ICP signal. Appear as a “cloud”
in the spectroram ﬁlling most of the frequency bandwidth.

Chapter 5
ICP Pattern Description
In collaboration with the Clinic of Neurosurgery, the proposed classiﬁcation protocol
are based on clinical experience and already described pressure waves by Lundberg and
Yokota. The protocol formulate the basis for the manual annotation of ICP pressure wave
patterns using the ICP-RC System.
At the beginning of the project period a seminar was held by the Clinic consisting
of co-supervisors and invited health care professionals with ICP experience. The goal of
the seminar was to discuss and form ICP pressure wave classes. The seminar resulted
in 13 diﬀerent ICP classes where some were very similar. The whiteboard drawings can
be found in Appendix F. The naming was numbered from 1 to 9 with a + or - to in-
dicate alternating appearance for the same class. This, however proved to be way too
ambitious and confusing after manually trying to annotate the classes in vers 1.1 of the
ICP-RC System. It was quickly discovered to be extremely diﬃcult for the neurologist
to consistently identify the same classes in diﬀerent ICP curves. Moreover did the poor
naming increase the confusion, leading to very high variation in the annotation between
the neurologists. In this chapter are the formation and description of the ﬁrst classiﬁcation
protocol presented.
5.1
Classiﬁcation Protocol
The ﬁrst protocol is a product of several evaluations and test runs with the ICP-RC
System.
After multiple iterations and meetings between the Clinic and DTU did the
ﬁrst version of the protocol include six main classes: Solid (Sol), Irregular (Irr), Regular
B-waves (RegB), Small A-like (SmA), Classic A (AA) and Large A-Like (LAL). The
classiﬁcation protocol have the following hypothesis and conditions:
1. Regular or repetitive pressure waves deviating from the background amplitude or
pattern is more pathological than equal pressure waves with an irregular pattern.
2. The amplitude is an important parameter regarding both “background” and super-
posed pathological pressure ﬂuctuations. The more the amplitude of a pressure wave
diﬀerentiates from the “background” the more pathological it is.
• There is a correlation between ICP and amplitude - Therefore are scoring based
on patterns prioritized.
• Quantiﬁcation of amplitude average, median or quartiles can be analyzed sec-
ondarily.
51
==PAGE:68==
52
CHAPTER 5. ICP PATTERN DESCRIPTION
Each class are described in greater detail along with illustrative examples to begin
standardization and consistency.
Amplitude variance and mean ICP will be used fre-
quently and is illustrated in ﬁgure 5.1.
Figure 5.1: Example ICP curve indicating mean ICP as the mean over a period of time
with a consistent pattern and amplitude as the variance of the ICP curve.
5.1.1
Solid Pattern – No Amplitude Deﬂections (Sol)
Very uniformly amplitude variance without any of the below described patterns. The heart
rate is recognized as background. Can occur in long uninterrupted sections (the normal
pattern), and as intermediate section/background in pathological curves overlaid with one
or more of the below pressure wave types. There is a positive correlation between mean
ICP and amplitude. Normal ICP is stated diﬀerent in the literature: Up to 15 mmHg in
supine position [2].
• Amplitude variation correlated to max 15 mmHg mean ICP has an amplitude vari-
ance less than 5 mmHg [19]
• Low mean ICP yields lower amplitude variation.
Practical Examples
A. Long uninterrupted uniform curve (Normal pattern) is shown in ﬁgure 5.2a
B. Intermediate/background in a pathological curve with other overlaying pathological
pressure wave types (ﬁgure 5.2b).
==PAGE:69==
5.1. CLASSIFICATION PROTOCOL
53
(a) Example of Solid curve that is uninterrupted for longer period of time. Found in
provided data.
(b) Example of solid curve that intermediate/background in a pathological sequence.
Found in provided data set.
5.1.2
Irregular Wave Pattern (Irr)
Irregular waves manifests as a varying non-repetitative pattern which can replace the Solid
pattern as basis ICP. Irregular waves are both of varying amplitude and duration. Appears
as a very irregular pattern where the heart rate is still recognizable as background. Mean
ICP can take any value.
• Amplitude variance > 5 mmHg can be:
– Small: 5-10 mmHg
– Large: >10 mmHg
– Mixed: where irregular basis pattern overlaid with single waves of higher amp-
litude.
Practical Examples
A. Long uninterrupted uniform curve where the Irregular pattern has replaced Solid as
basis pattern (ﬁgure 5.3a).
B. “Mixed” where Irregular basis pattern overlaid with single waves of higher amplitude
(ﬁgure 5.3b).
==PAGE:70==
54
CHAPTER 5. ICP PATTERN DESCRIPTION
(a) Example of irregular pattern that is uninterrupted for longer period of time and has
replaced solid as basis ICP. Found in provided data set.
(b) Example of irregular patterns mixed with larger single waves of higher amplitude.
Found in provided data set.
5.1.3
Regular B-wave Pattern (RegB)
Appears as a continuous repetitive pattern consisting of clearly separated single waves,
where the heart rate is recognized as background. Regular B-waves “rises” oscillating and
abrupt over the basis ICP with small variations in amplitude and duration giving a regular
appearance. Mean ICP can be all values but usually in the upper end of the normal range
(15 mmHg) or above.
• Amplitude > 5 mmHg, with the amplitude variation being
– Small: 5-10 mmHg
– Large: >10 mmHg
• Frequency: 1
2 −2 per min [36].
Practical Examples
A. Long uninterrupted regular B-wave curve (ﬁgure 5.4a).
B. Intermediate section with regular B-waves (ﬁgure 5.4b).
==PAGE:71==
5.1. CLASSIFICATION PROTOCOL
55
(a) Long section of pathological B-waves with clear oscillation and low variation in dur-
ation and amplitude of the individual pulses. Found in provided data set.
(b) Short section of regular B-waves transitioning into small A-like wave. Clear oscillation
and low variation in duration and amplitude of the individual pulses. Found in provided
data set.
5.1.4
Small A-like Wave Pattern (smA)
Small A-like waves has A-wave nature (see below) but is smaller in amplitude and duration.
• Mean ICP on plateau is 10-30 mmHg
• Duration max 20 min
• The plateau constitute the longest duration
• “Front” and “back” edge can have varying absolute and individual duration.
• The amplitude is cumulated single waves > 5 mmHg
Practical Examples
A. Figure 5.5a and 5.5b shows to examples of small A-like waves.
==PAGE:72==
56
CHAPTER 5. ICP PATTERN DESCRIPTION
(a) Example of small A-like waves, the mean ICP is below 30 mmHg and has a “back”
edge longer than 3 min. Found in provided data set.
(b) Small A-like that “rises” above basis ICP and has a plateau with an mean amplitude
less than 30 mmHg. Found in provided data set.
5.1.5
A-wave Pattern (AA)
Sections of several minutes (5 - 45 min) duration where the amplitude and mean ICP
gradually parallel increases. A–waves consist of an accumulation of large regular or irreg-
ular single waves that increases and builds up to a plateau. The plateau constitute the
majority of the duration thus the “front” and “back” edge are of maximal 3 minutes, with
the back edge being the steepest.
• Mean ICP of plateau is > 30 mmHg
• Amplitude is cumulated single waves > 10 mmHg [52]
Practical Examples
A. Figure 5.6a shows very distinct A-waves.
B. Figure 5.6b shows a combination of A and small A-like waves. A-waves are marked
with red and an explanatory text argues why it is not classiﬁed as small A-lik.
==PAGE:73==
5.1. CLASSIFICATION PROTOCOL
57
(a) A-wave with mean ICP at plateau greater than 30 mmHg and with a “front” and
“back” edge less than 3 min. The duration is in the interval of 5 - 45 min. Found in
provided data set.
(b) A-wave determined from the duration.
The arrow indicates a doubt case where
duration of the wave is less than 5 minutes and is therefore not classiﬁed as an A-wave.
If mean ICP at the plateau is max 30 mmHg it can be classiﬁed as “small A-like”. If
the mean ICP at the plateau is greater than 30 mmHg is it classiﬁed as large irregular
waves.
5.1.6
Large A-like Deﬂections (LAL)
Deﬂections of same magnitude as classic A but with diﬀerent appearance. The “front”
and “back” edge is longer than 3 min.1.
• Mean ICP of plateau > 30 mmHg
Practical Examples
A. Figure 5.7 shows very a type of Large A-Like wave. Notable diﬀerence from the A
is the longer “front” and “back” edge.

duration and sides in to each other. Large A-like not found in provided data explains the
rareness and change of diagram. Not found in provided data.

Chapter 6
Scored ICP Data and Review of
Classiﬁcation Protocol
The ICP data was scored at the Clinic using the ICP RC System and the classiﬁcation
protocol ending at a total of 14 measurements from 11 patients. Hereof was 8 measure-
ments from 6 patients scored by two neurologists (NS). The ﬁrst NS (NS1) scored the
data before the classiﬁcation protocol was completed but participated in the meetings
leading up to the protocol. The ICP-RC System only allows the NS to annotate 6 classes
described by the protocol. However the ICP signal is also prone to noise and artifact
elements that do not have a class button. The events was simply not annotated and will
be referred to as the “Artifact” class. Recall from chapter 4 that each class is assigned to
an integer value representing this class where 1: small A, 2: classic A, 3: Large A-Like,
4: Regular B, 5: Irregular and 6: Solid. The Artifact class will be assigned the value 0.
In this chapter are scored data and pre-processing explained. A group review of the
classiﬁcation protocol were held at the end of the project to further evaluate the protocol
for future improvement.
6.1
Data Description
Before using the scored data for the advanced machine learning in part II are the scored
raw data pre-processed regarding the following:
• Missing values
• Connect small annotation gaps
• Artifact class
6.1.1
Missing Values
Some of the scored ICP curves contained no measured value for unknown reasons. The
measurement entry was replaced by a NaN value in the raw data ﬁle. Diﬀerent methods
described by Theodoridis [49] is developed to handle missing values. One approach is
the replace the NaN with zeros or the unconditional mean computed from the pdf of the
class the missing values was observed in. Another and more rough approach is to simply
discard the missing values. The NaN for the provided data was mainly in the beginning
of end of the measurements and was therefore discarded even though it is considered to
be throwing away information.
59
==PAGE:76==
60
CHAPTER 6. SCORED ICP DATA AND REVIEW OF CLASSIFICATION
PROTOCOL
6.1.2
Annotation Gaps
Each time an event is annotated by NS manually marking the start and end of the ICP
event will imprecision cause small gaps between consecutive annotations.
Small gaps
marked by the red circles are shown in ﬁgure 6.1. Since artifacts in the signal also are
Figure 6.1: Examples of small gaps in annotated data
marked by not annotating will these gaps, if nothing is done, be put in the Artifact class
(discussed in next section) hence introducing noise to the classes. To avoid this problem
is a search algorithm implemented. The algorithm iterates through the scored data and
ﬁnds the zeros values and counts the number of consecutive zeros. If the number is less
than 100 samples (equivalent to 20 sec.) then the zeros are assigned to the ﬁrst non-zero
value following the gap. Implementation is executed by loadData in Appendix B.3.
6.1.3
The Artifact Class
The same search is done for the Artifact class, this time all gaps containing more than 100
consecutive zeros are kept at the value zero. Lastly artifacts with gaps longer than 200
min are considered to be NaN and discarded from the annotation list as well as in the ICP
signal. The above explained is executed by the loadData function found in AppendixB.3.
6.1.4
Annotated Data
After the preproccesing the total amount of data scored by NS1 are summarized in
table 6.1. Total time and percentage explains the distribution of each class along with
the number of measurements containing speciﬁc class. The same is shown in table 6.2 for
NS2. Note that the total time is given in DD:HH:MM and percentage of the total amount
of data is %. It is clearly seen that classes such as Irregular and Solid are strongly rep-
resented since these are considered to be normal or little pathological [52]1. Furthermore
all types of A-waves are very limited which will have an impact on the training of the
classiﬁer.
1Along with Yokotas description of Solid waves are the Irregular not described in the literature and
the assumption of little pathological severeness build on clinical experience.

Review of ICP Classiﬁcation Protocol
The scored data by the two neurologist were not only used for the machine learning but
also analyzed for agreement. This showed that the two neurologist only scores agreeably
66.6 % of the time (see the Results, chapter 10). For future beneﬁts were the classiﬁcation
protocol evaluated by a group review where several ICP curves were printed out as seen in
ﬁgure 6.2. The print outs were folded so the spectrogram and ICP was separated (marked
by the black dashed line). The task was to score the ICP curves again in two iterations.
1. Score the data only on the spetrogram
2. Score the data only on the analog ICP curve
The scoring were ten compared. The purpose was to:
1. Get the neurologist to become more acquainted with the spectrogram and the ap-
pearance in the presences of various patterns.
2. Test the neurologists consistency.
3. Challenge the classes in the classiﬁcation protocol.
After the group scoring by the neurologists were the individual scoring displayed for the
same ICP. This guided the discussion and evaluation of the current classiﬁcation protocol.

6.2.1
Output
The output of the review is in summary:
• Identiﬁcation of troublesome classes determined to be the Irregular class since this
class were scored both as Solid and Regular B.
• Redeﬁnition of the multiple A wave classes - a possible amalgamation of the Large
A-like and classic A patterns.
• For future work; do another iteration where an uncertainty input can be given to
each scoring. E.g 1-3 where 1: certain, 2: almost certain and 3: Uncertain. This
could help in the machine learning process.
• For future work; do reversed scoring by using unsupervised learning to let data create
clusters and see if these have diagnostic meaning.

found in Appendix L.

Chapter 7
Feature Generation and Selection
The main inspiration for computed features bases on what neurologist are looking at in
their manual inspection of the ICP curves. Other features are found in the literature of
more researched areas like EEG, EMG and Speech recognition. The number of features
produced for the classiﬁcation system can be quite large which can lead to the curse of
dimensionality which can lead to a decrease in classiﬁer performance. Selecting discrim-
inative features have multiple advantages [27] [49].
1. General data reduction, reduces storage requirements and increase algorithm speed.
2. feature set reduction, some features may carry good class information when treated
separately but little is gained when set in combination with others due to high
mutual correlation.
3. performance improvement, removal of irrelevant even noisy feature and selection of
strong descriptive features will result in better generalization capabilities.
In this chapter will generated features be summarized, followed by feature selection and
classiﬁer validation methods.
7.1
Feature Generation
A total of 45 features are computed and summarized in table 7.1 and 7.2. Abbreviations
are used in generated MATLAB functions located in Appendix B.3. Time domain features
described by the neurologist, and mentioned in the classiﬁcation protocol focuses primarily
on mean, variance, standard deviation, peak to peak and max/min values of the ICP curve.
Most literature describe using Fourier Transform (FT) of the ICP signal as features [28].
Short Term Fourier Transform is used to get the FT content of the epochs.
Wavelet
Transform with Debauchies mother wavelet to get a better time-frequency resolution.
Feature equation and description are found in Appendix A.

7.1.1
Epochs
Using epochs on the ICP signal are based on the assumption that the ICP signal within
a short period of time is considered to be the same (quasi-stationary) and therefore be
representative for the signal [43]. The epoch length are selected based one State of The
Art studies and ICP wave types described in the classiﬁcation protocol. Since little has
been researched regarding optimal epochs of ICP waves except for García et. al. [25].
Solid ICP waves is not varying and will not diﬀer signiﬁcantly from two epoch lengths.
Regular B and A-waves have very slow changes ranging from 1
2 to 2 min and 5 to 45
==PAGE:83==
7.2. FEATURE NORMALIZATION
67
min respectively [36]. Small epochs will be very prone to artifacts and not fully capture
the slow variation. Especially time domain features could suﬀer from small epochs when
found in e.q the slope of an A-wave or B-wave resulting in a Solid like appearance. Hare
et al. used 30 sec epoch on recorded ICP sampled at 128 Hz. Available ICP is only 5 Hz
and epochs on 5 min epoch are chosen with 75% overlap for all generated features. The
overlap is selected to compensate for the poor time resolution of the 5 min epochs.
7.2
Feature Normalization
The computed features will have diﬀerent dynamic ranges and larger values may have a
greater inﬂuence on the classiﬁer than smaller which doesn’t necessarily have to be the
best feature. Hence the features must be normalized to have the same dynamic range [49].
A popular choice is standardization given by:

7.3
Feature Selection
Not all computed features will beneﬁt to a good general classiﬁcation. Some features will
have better generalization properties, thus the task at hand is to ﬁnd the optimal feature
subset that gives the best general performance. The optimal approach would be to try
all available feature subset combinations. However this leads to a considerable amount of
combinations given by:
(m, l) =
m!
l!(m −l)!
==PAGE:84==
68
CHAPTER 7. FEATURE GENERATION AND SELECTION
Having l = 10 best features from a total of m = 45 features will result in 3.2 · 109 possible
combinations which is very time consuming and impossible in practice [49]. Common and
often used methods for SVM are forward and backward feature selection which are simple
to implement and produce reliable results [48] [49] [12] [16].
7.3.1

Sequential feature selection oﬀers a great reduction in combinations due to the way of
selecting feature subset [49]. The two methods are very similar and are described by the
following steps in table 7.3. The two methods are graphically illustrated in ﬁgure 7.1.

substantially less than that of the full search.

A limitation of the backward features selection approach is that the optimal subset is not
guaranteed to be the best subset since it is stopped at ﬁrst convex, where in fact a smaller
feature subset actually is better [49]. Forward feature selection can chose a feature that
alone has high discriminating abilities but in combination with other features results in
lower performance than diﬀerent subset. Forward feature selection is suggested by Hsu et
al. [12] when working with SVM.
7.3.2
Bhattacharyya Distance
The steps for the sequential forward feature selection in table 7.3 are implemented by
featureSelection in Appendix B.6.
The criteria for the forward feature selection is
based on the Bhattacharyya Distance. The Bhattacharyya Distance is a class separability
measure computing the distance between 2 feature groups by using the mean (µ) and
co-variance matrix (Σ) [49].

The feature yielding the largest distance is selected and kept out for next iteration. The
distance is yet again computed for the remaining features and the new feature with largest
distance are selected. The procedure is repeated for all features resulting in a distance
vector of which a subset of feature is chosen (chapter 9).
7.4
Cross-Validation
Cross validation is a method to evaluate the performance of a classiﬁer [48]. The idea
is to split a complete data set in to two data sets. One data set is used for training a
classiﬁer (training) and then tested on the other data set (test). Common methods are
k-fold and leave-one-out. In k-fold are the complete data set divided in to k groups where
the classiﬁer is trained on k-1 training sets and tested on the last unseen data set. k-fold
is best when there is a suﬃcient amount of data to train on and will result in multiple
evaluation of the classiﬁer, where a mean can be used as a more general indication of the
expected performance of the classiﬁer. If there are a limited amount of data available, the
leave-one-out method is a more popular choice [48]. The leave-one-out method holds out
a complete data set to test on and trains on the remaining. Then the data set is put back
and another data set is taken out and the classiﬁer is trained and tested again with the
new data set leaved out. Methods are schematically illustrated in ﬁgure 7.2 [48].
==PAGE:86==
70
CHAPTER 7. FEATURE GENERATION AND SELECTION
Figure 7.2: Schematic illustration of the k-fold holdout and leave-one-out method.
7.4.1
Performance Measure
As described by Warby et al. [51] are several performance measures available to validate
classiﬁcation. A commonly accepted way to display classiﬁer performance is the confusion
matrix as depicted in ﬁgure 7.3. Considering a binary classiﬁcation problem are True
Positive (TP) the predicted positive that actual is positive. True Negative (TN) predicted
negative that are actual negative. False Positive (FP) predicted positive but are actual
negative (type I error). False Negative (FN) predicted positive but are actual negative
(type II error).
The confusion matrix shows the classiﬁers ability to correctly predict
events compared to a golden standard that is considered valid. A popular performance
measure are the Accuracy given by:
Accuracy = TP + TN
P + N
(7.6)
Where P and N are the total number of Positive and Negative classes respectively. Other
known measures are sensitivity, speciﬁcity and precision. Sensitivity also known as recall
or True Positive Rate (TPR) provides a fraction of predicted classes that is actual that
Figure 7.3: Confusion matrix visualizing classiﬁer performance. True Positive (TP): Pre-
dicted positive and actual positive. True Negative (TN): Predicted negative ans actual
negative. False Positive (FP): Predicted Positive but actual negative (type I error). False
Negative (FN): Predicted positive but actual negative (type II error).
==PAGE:87==
7.4. CROSS-VALIDATION
71
class, given by:
sensitivity =
TP
TP + FN
(7.7)
Precision is a complementary performance measure to the sensitivity since precision is a
proportion of the predicted classes that are correct.
precision =
TP
TP + FP
(7.8)
A high sensitivity can capture all the TP but at the risk of getting to many FP which is
undesirable. A high sensitivity with a high precision is more desirable since this shows
that most of the predicted classes are also correct.
The F1score is a measure of this
combination given by:
F1score = 2 · sensitivity · precision
sensitivity + precision
(7.9)
F1score will be used over Accuracy since the rare case classes will not be captured by the
accuracy. This is better understood by an example. In many real life problems such as
mammography are the rare case very important to successfully predict. Consider the a
classiﬁer predicting 100 events with 97 predicted as TN and 3 as FP yielding the following
confusion matrix shown in ﬁgure 7.4. The Accuracy, Sensitivity, Precision and F1score is
Figure 7.4: Confusion matrix of the rare class example. Showing an classiﬁer being very
good at predicting TN but very poor at predicting TP.
in table 7.4.
Table 7.4: Performance measures from example
Perfom. Measure
Value
Accuracy
97%
Sensitivity
0 %
Precision
0 %
F1score
0 %
It is clear that if the goal is to predict TN the Accuracy is the performance measure to use,
but if the rare case is of interest then accuracy will give a false indication of the classiﬁers
ability to predict the desired class since sensitivity and precision are 0%. Therefore will a
sensitivity-precision plot or F1score be a more adequate performance measure.

Chapter 8
Automatic Classiﬁcation of
Annotated ICP Signals
Today a huge variety of classiﬁcation methods are available ranging from simple such as
decision tree or k-nearest-neighbor to more advanced like Neural Network (NN), Hidden
Markow Models (HMM) or Support Vector Machine (SVM) [6] [49]. HMM is widely used
for key word spotting in speech recognition [32] [50]. Both NN and SVM has shown to
perform very well in many EEG applications [47] [29].
Since classifying ICP patterns
haven’t been tried it is unsure which classiﬁer will be the best choice. However, SVM
performs well in general and can handle large number of features [49]. Based on these
properties, the SVM method is selected for the classiﬁcation task. In this chapter “Pattern
Recognition” by Theodoridis [49], “Pattern Recognition and Machine Learning used” by C.
M. Bishop [6] and MIT open course [40] are used to explain the Support Vector Machine
in greater detail with key equations and concepts. Additional remarks will be cited as
normal.
8.1
Support Vector Machine
The SVM is by nature a two-class (binary) classiﬁer, which works by searching for an
optimal decision boundary between features that can completely separate the two classes.
Consider the data points of two classes in ﬁgure 8.1a. The SVM will draw a straight line
or hyperplane in such a way that there is the furthest perpendicular distance from the
line to the data points. This approach is call Maximum Margin Hyperplane.
(a)
(b)
Figure 8.1: Imaginary data set consisting of two features. Red dots are data point be-
longing to class I (t1) and blue dots belong to class II (t2).
73
==PAGE:90==
74 CHAPTER 8. AUTOMATIC CLASSIFICATION OF ANNOTATED ICP SIGNALS
8.1.1
Maximum Margin Hyperplane
Linearly Separable
Consider the two-class classiﬁcation problem with a given training data set (X) consisting
of N features x1, ..., xN belonging to class I (t1) or class II (t2). It’s assumed that the two
classes are linearly separable. The output, g(x) is the classiﬁed label from new input data.
The goal is to design a hyperplane g(x) that completely separates the two classes. The
hyperplane given by:
g(x) = wT x + b = 0
(8.1)
Where wT is a weight vector which determines the direction of the hyperplane and b
deﬁnes the position in space. Many hyperplanes will qualify to completely separate the
two classes as seen in ﬁgure 8.1b.
As illustrated in ﬁgure 8.2b some hyperplanes are better than others. In the given example
one would pick hyperplane 1 over 2 and 3 since it separates the two classes with the highest
equal distance to the nearest data points of both classes. This leaves the most “room”
for unseen data points to move in any direction and still be classiﬁed correctly. The two
other hyperplanes (2 and 3) are not as ﬂexible even though they also completely separates
the two classes and also has a high distance from the plane surface to one of the classes.
Therefore an constraint is added that the hyperplane must be placed in such a way that
the perpendicular distance from the surface to the closest data points of both classes are
equal and maximized as depicted in ﬁgure 8.2a.
(a)
(b)
Figure 8.2: Margin is deﬁned as the perpendicular distance between the decision boundary
(red line) and the closest data points in class I (y = 1) and class II (y = -1).
The goal is to search for the direction that gives the maximum possible margins as
shown in ﬁgure 8.2b. The distance from a point to the surface of the hyperplane is given
by.
z = |g(x)|
||w|| = |wT x + b|
||w||
(8.2)
w and b can now be scaled so values of g(x) to the nearest data points in class t1,t2 are
equal to 1 for t1 and equal to -1 for t2. This is given by:
1. Having a margin of:

yi(wT x + b) ≥1

8.1.2
Non-Separable
So far we are able to ﬁnd and classify two classes that are linearly separable. However,
in many real life problems data would rarely be so well separated and an overlap between
the two classes are very likely. Consider an other example depicted in ﬁgure 8.4, where
two classes are described by feature 1 and 2. This time the two classes are no longer
completely separable and training a SVM classiﬁer will not be able to linearly separate
the two classes linearly in two dimensions as seen in ﬁgure 8.4.
Figure 8.4: Computed hyperplane with optimal margins with minimal data points inside.
Slack variables ξ for diﬀerent data points determined by the distance from margin to point.
It is impossible to create a hyperplane where no data points would be inside the two
margins. The scenario for the feature vectors are:
• Vectors that fall outside the margin are correctly classiﬁed.
==PAGE:94==
78 CHAPTER 8. AUTOMATIC CLASSIFICATION OF ANNOTATED ICP SIGNALS
• Vectors falling inside the margins and are correctly classiﬁed are satisfying the in-
equality
0 ≤yi(wT x + b < 1
• Vectors that are misclassiﬁed that is those on the opposite side of the hyperplane to
their class. They satisfy the inequality
yi(wT x + b < 0
To treating all of the above cases under a single constraint is done by introducing a slack
variable ξi. The ﬁrst case corresponds to ξi = 0, the second 0 < ξi ≤1 and the third
ξi > 1 as shown in ﬁgure 8.4. The optimization task remains the same, but the goal now is
to make the margins as large as possible and at the same time keep the number of points
with ξ > 0 as small as possible. Putting this into the objective function eq. 8.15 yields:
J(w, b, ξ) = 1
2||w||2 + C
N
X
i=1
I(ξi)
(8.16)
Where C is a positive constant that controls the relative inﬂuence of the two competing
terms. ξ is the vector of parameters ξi and
I(ξi) =
(

ξi > 0
0
ξi = 0

end up with:

The Lagrange multipliers correspond to the points residing either within the margin or on
the wrong side of the hyperplane. ξi > 0 are all equal to the maximum allowable value C.
This means that the points having the largest possible “share” in the ﬁnal solution of w.
To further reduce the number of overlapping data points a neat trick comes into play.
Instead of trying to ﬁnd the best hyperplane in the same feature dimension, we now map
the existing features in l-dimensional feature space to a higher k-dimensional features
space. Here the two classes could be more separable by the hyperplane. From the above
section the optimal hyperplane is found by:
g(x) =
Ns
X
i=1
λiyixiT x + b
(8.18)
Where g(x) ≥±1 depends x belonging to class I or II. Ns are the number of support
vectors. From before the decision is still only dependent on the inner product between
xiT x. The only diﬀerence is now that the dot product of the two vectors takes place in
k’th dimensional space produced by the mapping of the original vectors. The mapping can
be expressed as a function of inner products of the corresponding vectors in the original
==PAGE:95==
8.1. SUPPORT VECTOR MACHINE
79
feature space. This mapping will be denoted, φ(x). Since it is only the dot product that
determines the hyperplane then we can write the transformation function (K) as:
K(xi, xj) = (φ(xi)φ(xj))
(8.19)
Functions where there exists a space in which K(xi, xj) deﬁnes an inner product are called
Kernels and mapping the data points to a higher feature dimension is known as the Kernel
trick. The Kernel trick is illustrated in ﬁgure 8.5.

higher dimensional feature space were linear separation is possible. Created in MATLAB

Note that most of these kernel function are non-linear meaning that the decision hyper-
plane is no longer linear. Adjusting parameters C, σ and γ, also called hyperparameters are
needed to ﬁne-tune the SVM’s decision boundary and margins to achieve better classiﬁc-
ation performance [12]. Choosing the optimal kernel function is still an unsolved research
area and is still not solved [49]. The best kernel depends on provided data and is often
done experimentally. A common ﬁrst choice is the Radial Basis Functions (RBF) [49].
8.2
Multiclass SVM
Because of SVM being a binary classiﬁer certain approaches can be done when dealing
with multiclass problems. A common approach is “one-versus-rest”, where K individual
SVM models are trained on data belonging to k’th class. The k’th class will be positive
and all other K-1 classes negative. Hence classifying the k’th class using the trained SVM
against the rest. This is repeated for all K classes and individual SVM. The drawbacks of
this approach is:
• More of the same regions can be classiﬁed as 1 (belonging to k’th class).
• The training set is imbalanced since say, there are 10 classes each with an equal
amount of data points. When individual classiﬁers are trained they will only be
trained on 10% of the data and thereby losing some of the symmetry of the original
problem and will cause the SVM to “favor” the majority class.
Another popular approach is the “one-versus-one” where K(K−1)
2
diﬀerent 2-class SVM
models are trained and each classiﬁer separates a pair of classes of all possible pairs. The
decision is made on the basis of a majority vote. The disadvantage is that a relatively
large number of SVM models need to be trained.
Rare Classes
In many cases are the desired class not adequately represented, e.g in mammography will
the majority of scans contain nothing, leaving the True Negative class to become very
large. When trying to ﬁt an optimal decision boundary will the SVM “favor” the majority
class since more support vectors are available and the trade-oﬀwith data points inside he
margin will be low if placed close to the minority class. This is exempliﬁed in ﬁgure 8.6.
==PAGE:97==
8.3. LIBSVM
81
(a)
(b)
Figure 8.6: a) showing medium major class (-) and minor class (+), The SVM decision
boundary slightly favors the majority class. This is more evident in b, were a large majority
class is heavily favored.
Several approaches deals with this problem [10]. One approach described by Kubat et
al. [33] selectively under-sample the majority class while keeping the original population
of the minority class.
A more advance technique is suggested by Ling et al. [35] to
use combined random over-sampling of the minority class with under-sampling of the
majority class. In the same line is a third method proposed by Chawla et al. [10] called
Synthetic Minority Over-sampling TEchnique. SMOTE uses k-nearest neighbors to create
more samples of the minority class by taking each minority class sample and introducing
synthetic examples along the line segments joining any/all of the k minority class nearest
neighbors and thereby create samples within the existing sample group.
8.3
LIBSVM
MATLAB oﬀers a build-in implementation of SVM which can be used directly in MAT-
LAB. LIBSVM made by Chang and Lin [11] is an open source SVM application made for
MATLAB and other programming tools and is available at http://ntucsu.csie.ntu.
edu.tw/~cjlin/libsvm/. LIBSVM solves the quadratic programming (QP) optimization
problem in eq. 8.20 by using Sequential Minimal Optimization (SMO) decomposition ap-
proach. SMO sets the decomposition to its extreme where each pair of QP problems only
consists of two Lagrange multipliers. This means that the optimization can be solved
analytically, which avoids using a time-consuming numerical QP optimization.
Above
mentioned kernel functions are available and described in [23].
==PAGE:98==
==PAGE:99==
Chapter 9
Design of Classiﬁer
The Support Vector Machine described in chapter 8 is used in the following to design the
SVM classiﬁer. Each SVM model is designed through 4 steps suggested by Hsu et al. [12]:
1. Split the complete data set up into a training and test set.
2. Select the best feature subset for each class (feature selection, chapter 7).
3. Find the optimal hyperparameters for each class (SVM chapter 8).
4. Train a SVM model for each class (SVM chapter 8).
The normal range of ICP is between -10 and 15 mmHg where the three most common
classes (Solid, Irregular and Regular) can occur (see classiﬁcation protocol, chapter 6).
Some patients have a mean ICP above 15 mmHg and still have ICP patterns with Solid
appearance. This could be confused with the plateau of A-waves and could be classiﬁed
as such. In the following chapter, the above steps will be performed on ICP data with no
signal modiﬁcation, referred to as Raw and on Standardized ICP data given by eq. 7.1
referred to as Standardized. The hypothesis is that classiﬁcation on Standardized ICP
data will perform better than on Raw ICP.
9.1
Choose Training and Test Data
To validate the ﬁnal classiﬁer a small data set is chosen to be completely removed from
the data used to train and build the SVM model. Test data is used to evaluate the ﬁnal
SVM models on in the Result chapter 10. Two neurologists managed to manually score
the ICP data, they will for convenience be referred to as “NS1” and “NS2”. NS1 managed
to score 14 patients in total. From the 14 are three patients randomly selected to be the
test data, hence leaving the rest as training data. The three patients should ensure that
all the classes are represented in the test data. NS2 only scored a total of 8 patients.
Taking three patients will leave the classiﬁer with very little data to train on. This leaves
the option of taking only one patient out, however taking one patient out could contain
only 1 or 2 of the seven classes and then not be representative for all classes. By removing
a patient containing the majority of the rare case classes such as A-waves will remove im-
portant training data for the model. Instead 1
8 of each patient is taken out and combined
into a new test patient. By doing so the test data will be able to catch most of the classes
and still not remove important training data. Lastly combining the scoring of both NS is
tried. The data set is generated by taking all instances where the two NS have scored the
same and put them into one “patient”. The test data is created by taking 1
4 of the data set
83

4 as training. The training and test for the diﬀerent scoring approaches

9.2
Feature Selection
To solve the multiclass problem the one-versus-rest approach is used due to computational
ease. Several binary SVM models must be made for each class and each will have an
optimal subset of features. The Bhattacharyya distance is computed for each class using
the sequential forward features approach. Figure 9.1 depicts the Bhattacharyya distance
for all 7 classes Solid, Irregular, Regular, Large A-Like, Classic A, small A and Artifact
from NS1 on raw data. The number of features are selected based on the appearance of
the line. If the curve has a knee point as seen in ﬁgure 9.1e-g meaning that the following
features do not provide a large amount of separability to that class, and will therefore not
be selected. In cases where no knee-point is visible and takes the appearance of a straight
line, is half of the features selected as seen in ﬁgure 9.1a and b. A third case is seen in
ﬁgure 9.1c and d when dealing with the rare classes such as “A”, “LAL” and “small A”
there will not be suﬃcient amount of data to proper compute the distance and therefore
will discard them leaving only a few features actually providing separability - in those
cases all features are included.

9.2.1
Hyperparameter Search
After ﬁnding a better feature subset the explained hyperparameters C and γ are important
to modify in order to ﬁne-tune the SVM models. Recall from chapter 8 are C the penalty
hyperparameter of the error term and γ deﬁnes the width of the RBF kernel. The LIBSVM
library deﬁnes the RBF kernel as [23]:
K(xi, xj) = eγ||xi−xj||2
As suggested by Hsu et al. [12] the hyperparameter search are performed as a grid search
using cross validation to select optimal hyperparameters.
C and γ are selected on a
2n base to increase computational speed. The grid are set to C = [2−2, 20, ..., 218] and
γ = [2−10, 2−8..., 20]. The hyperparameters for the rare class will be somewhat sparse since
there are patients with no event and thus performance parameters can not be calculated.
The hyperparameters are found for all patients with leave-one-out as the cross-validation
method. This is repeated for the Raw and Standardized training data sets of NS1, NS2
and NS1+2.
The optimal hyperparameters are evaluated by using F1score computed
for each C and γ followed by taking the mean. The largest mean F1score is computed
and corresponding C and γ values are selected as the best hyperparameter. Naturally
this is not the optimal hyperparameters for the individual patient but it gives the best
overall performance across all patients. The hyperparameter search of four classes on Raw
ICP data scored by NS1 is shown in ﬁgure 9.2. Under-represented classes can result in
0 sensitivity and precision and yields a NaN when computed as seen in ﬁgure 9.2a and
b.
More balanced classes will more likely be able to predict the classes correctly and
will ﬁll up the grid as seen in ﬁgure 9.2c and d.
The implementation is executed by
parameterSelection in Appendix B.6.
==PAGE:104==
88
CHAPTER 9. DESIGN OF CLASSIFIER
(a)
(b)
(c)
(d)
Figure 9.2: hyperparameter search of four class types on Raw ICP signal scored by NS1.
a) and b) shows a highly imbalanced data set having with C and γ values which results
in nothing found leading to a NaN value when computing the F1score. c) and d) shows
more balanced classes where performance are evaluated for the whole grid.
9.2.2
Multi Classiﬁcation
To ﬁnally test the combined SVM model on the test data the prediction of each binary
SVM is collected in one matrix. All instances, where only one of the classes is classiﬁed as
1, will be labeled with that speciﬁc class. In the situation where a section is classiﬁed as
multiple classes the LIBSVM packages provides an probability estimate on the likelihood
or “sureness” of the prediction. The class with the highest probability is selected and
labeled. The probability estimate is based on Platts scaling that uses a sigmoid function
to map the SVM outputs into probabilities [34].
9.2.3
Final Classiﬁer
To summaries above process the ﬁnal SVM model for each class is shown in table 9.3 and
9.4 for the Raw and Standardized data set scored by NS1. For overview purpose only
the 10 ﬁrst selected features are included. Same procedure is used for the NS2 and the
consensus NS1+2.

Chapter 10
Results
The represented results generated from the acquired data are scored by the two neurolo-
gists (NS1 and NS2) at the Clinic of Neurosurgery. The results includes the agreement
variation between neurologists along with the SVM performance. The SVM models used
to generate the results have the hyperparameters (C,γ) and features described and found
in chapter 9. Results of several test patients are mainly average to show the overall per-
formance. Individual patient performance values to generate the average values are found
in Appendix J. All performance results are followed by practical examples from a selec-
tion of patient ICP curves showing annotated ICP curves. Confusion matrices will help to
understand eventual misclassiﬁcations and is computed from epochs in stead of samples.
10.1
Interscore Variability
The agreement between the two NS is found by comparing the 8 scored patients and
compute the accuracy, sensibility, precision and F1-score. The overall agreement is shown
in Table10.1. The sensitivity and precision with SD for individual classes are shown in
table 10.2.

Practical Examples
Further investigation of the high variation in sensitivity and precision between the NS are
shown in practical examples in ﬁgure 10.1 and 10.2. Examples shows the same section of
an ICP curve with individual annotations from NS1, NS2 and the described group review.
All review examples and associated annotations are found in Appendix L. In ﬁgure 10.1
NS1 scores the whole ICP curve to be Regular B waves (yellow), whereas NS2 scores the
same ICP curve as Regular B (yellow), small A (light red), Irregular (green) and even
solid (blue) at the end. At the review both NS, as team, scored the same ICP curve to
be Irregular (green) until the large peak, which is an artifact and also clearly recognized
in all images.
In ﬁgure 10.2 NS1 score most of the ICP curve as Solid (blue) and a little to be Regular
B waves (yellow). NS2 scores the majority of the curve as Irregular (green) with some
Solid (blue) parts. At the review both NS scores all as Irregular. Both examples shows
very clear and strong disagreement between the two neurologists and further emphasized
by the third scoring at the review where both NS arrived at a third classiﬁcation. Lastly
ﬁgure 10.3 shows an example on complete agreement between NS1, NS2 and review. Even
though the whole ICP curve is annotated as solid with an Artifact present as indicated
in the bottom image. The reason for the poor spectrogram in the last image is due to
scanning of the print out for the review.

SVM Performance
The generated SVM models are tested on unseen data and combined to multi-class SVM
classiﬁer using probabilistic estimates when multiple classes agrees. The multi-class SVM
performance are compared in the following way:
1. SVM tested on data scored by NS1 with three unseen test patients both with Raw
and Standardized ICP data.
2. SVM tested on data scored by NS2 with one unseen test patient both Raw and
Standardized ICP data.
3. SVM tested on data from consensus NS1+2 with one unseen test patient both Raw
and Standardized ICP data.
Confusion matrices for individual patients for both Raw and Standardized ICP data can
be found in Appendix J

Observed in all the test patients no Regular B waves are classiﬁed correctly. For test

also classiﬁed as solid both in test patient 1,2 and 3 which indicates a large overlap of the
Irregular class in the Solid and Regular class. Naturally this will give high performance
when scoring a class dominated by Irregular patterns as in test patient 3.

rare classes and the lack of training data is most likely the reason for this.

the extreme over-representation.

Furthermore the F1score with 43.4% ±1.77 for standardization and 44.4% ±3.27 for raw
equal in performance (p = 0.67) is showing that there is nothing gain from standardizing
the ICP data before using machine learning.

==PAGE:123==
Chapter 11
Discussion
11.1
Part I
11.1.1
Data Acquisition and ICP-RC System
Due to the relative short time allocated for the thesis, the ICP-RC system only developed
to an extent where the software was functional enough for the intended use. Some func-
tions and design implementations are not optimal and should be improved or changed
for future work.
Installing the ICP-RC System on computers at the Clinic proved to
be challenging and was solved by installing MATLAB on a Clinic laptop using authors
license. This is a very problematic and short term solution. The ICP-RC System should
ultimately be developed in an open source programming language like JAVA. The total
amount of scored ICP data by the ﬁrst Neurologists was very unbalanced with the Arti-
fact, small A, Classic A and Large A-like classes accounting for less than 2% of the total
data scored by one neurologist and less than 3% scored by the second neurologist. This
caused considerable classiﬁcation problems when performing machine learning and showed
the importance of having an appropriate amount of each class.
The rare class problem was not attempted to be solved by the SMOTE algorithm. The
reason for this is due to the interscore variability between the neurologists, only agreeing
66.6% of the time, hence creating artiﬁcial replication of an unstable class is meaningless.
The review session further revealed a high intrascore variation of the individual neurologist
which is essential to be improved before continuing machine learning. Next iteration of
annotations should include a certainty index from 1-3, 1 being certain and 3 uncertain.
Implementing a certainty index will primarily enhance the understanding of each class
and secondly improve the training of the SVM models.
11.1.2
Classiﬁcation Protocol
The classiﬁcation protocol is a ﬁrst attempt to describe ICP patterns. The protocol was
formed from already described ICP patterns in the literature by Lundberg [36], Yokota [52]
and Eide [18], and clinical experience. However, even with the protocol, a high scoring
disagreement was observed between the two neurologists. Analysis of the results and the
group review identiﬁed the Irregular class to be mainly misclassiﬁed. The misclassiﬁed
classes included both Solid and Regular B and sometimes small A waves. With Irregu-
lar being a new class, further analysis and description is needed. A ﬁrst step could be
to modify the ICP-RC System to only allow annotation of Irregular patterns and put
the rest as a “garbage” class. To the annotation could a certainty-index be assigned by
107
==PAGE:124==
108
CHAPTER 11. DISCUSSION
the neurologist to indicate their sureness of the annotation. Analyzing these results will
provide additional characteristics and description for the Irregular class.
A consideration could be to merge small A, classic A and Large-A-Like into one single
class, since all are pathological and all account for less than 2% of total scored data.
Leading this into a two step classiﬁcation by; ﬁrst letting the SVM predict the new A
class (consisting of the 3 current A-classes) and then computing a mean ICP for all the
predicted A classes. A high mean ICP would indicate many classic A- or Large A-Like
waves, thus still providing valuable information about the patient’s status.
The high interscore variability is a serious matter since ICP is used in treatment and
diagnostics and calls for improvement. A way to reduce the inter- and intrascoring vari-
ation should include a revision of the classiﬁcation protocol followed by a new iteration of
manual annotation. An update to the protocol should include description of the spectro-
gram as done in chapter 5 which will provide additional information to the ICP patterns
and could be an extensive improvement to the protocol, thus an enhancement of the
manual scoring.
11.2
Part II
11.2.1
Performance Results
The results for the SVM showed poor, but consistent overall performance with a F1score
and accuracy of 43.4% ±1.77 and 42.4% ±1.45 respectively for Standardized ICP data,
and 44.4% ±3.27 and 36.3% ±2.9 for Raw. There were no evident (p=0.051) gains by
standardizing the ICP data before computing the features. The hypothesis for this still
stands as some patients have a mean ICP above 15 mmHg while others have below 0
mmHg and all have the same classes at both levels, which could introduce noise to the
classiﬁer. A diﬀerent approach is to remove the mean and percentile as features, even
though these features are prominent for particularly the Artifact, classic A and Large A-
like class and could reduce performance. Results also showed a high disagreement between
the two neurologists mainly around the three dominating classes Regular, Irregular and
Solid with sensitivities of 64.1% ±42.4, 41.8% ±34.6 and 76.7% ±34.2 and precision at
75.6% ±36.0, 68.9% ±34.5, and 70.3% ±21.3 respectively for each class. IIn other areas,
these parameters are above 60% in at least one or both and with a considerable lower
SD [51]. Same study had F1score values of 75±6% which shows a markedly higher agree-
ment and consistency. Before attempting a second try of machine learning, such values
should be achieved.
A last try was performed on the consensus data where 5-fold cross validation was used
to ﬁnd optimal hyperparameters repeated 5 times. The hyperparameters were used to
classify the same data using the 5 fold method. Despite the relative small data set did
this result in a promising accuracy of 84.8% ±1.9 and F1score of 61.5%. Nothing can be
concluded since there is no data that has been completely unseen as is the case for all
other results. This only shows trends that more consistent scoring could result in better
scoring which supports former discussion on the need for improved scoring.
Practical examples were used to investigate misclassiﬁed episodes revealing that SVM
heavily favored the Irregular class. Same class introduced complications for the neurolo-
gists which is a consequence of training on a class that highly overlaps with two closely
==PAGE:125==
11.2. PART II
109
related classes (Solid and Regular B). In some episodes the SVM did classify a section as
one class where neurologists annotated several, when indeed the ICP signal from a signal
processing point of view was consistent. It can be argued that the SVM is equally correct
in such situations.
An interesting attempt could be to perform unsupervised learning
to create clusters and then train and classify based on the clusters and evaluate their
diagnostic meaning.
11.2.2
Feature Generation and Selection
Selecting feature based on visual inspection is not the best way to select the optimal
amount of features, but should be a future improvement. A way to ﬁnd the optimal para-
meters is to automatically ﬁnd the knee point of the curve based on the diﬀerence between
consecutive features and set a threshold for when to stop.
The poor SVM performance is solely not linked to the relatively high Interscore viab-
ility of the neurologist. Generated features may not be optimal and should be constantly
searched and reviewed. A pre-step to the feature selection could be to roughly search for
redundant features by statistically hypothesis testing all features and remove those not
signiﬁcantly diﬀerent [49].
11.2.3
General Propositions
In this thesis was a rather long epoch (5 min) with a 75% overlap chosen. The length and
overlap was based on the described events in the literature aiming to capture the diﬀerent
ICP events and still keep a reasonable time resolution. Finding optimal epoch lengths and
overlap is an important task, but was not tried in order to keep some parameters ﬁxed
when evaluating the SVM.
Filtering was not used, even though studies showed improvement after using lowpass ﬁl-
tering [7]. This was not performed due to the low sampling frequency (5 Hz) and ﬁltering
would remove information in the frequency domain that already is quite sparse. As for
the epoch length, ﬁltering is a parameter that should be researched but was kept ﬁxed to
reduce the amount of parameters inﬂicting the SVM evaluation.
The majority of the ICP curves suﬀer from Artifacts shown as sudden high peaks for a very
short time (a few samples) rising from the basis ICP and back. In the scoring process was
some of these artifact marked while others were ignored which introduced some noise to
the annotated classes. Implementing a pre-processing step to remove clear artifact would
improve scoring, visual assessment and the SVM classiﬁcation. The artifact removal must
be done in collaboration with the Clinic since the artifact removal must not decrease any
diagnostic information.
Other classiﬁer modalities could be considered, even though SVM is believed to be one
of the top classiﬁer modalities today. Since the protocol builds on strict deﬁnitions on
amplitude level, variance and duration. A decision tree combined with a SVM could be a
strong alternative, where the decision tree works like a ﬁlter for the SVM model, ensuring
the ICP patterns falls in to better deﬁned categories based on the protocol deﬁnitions.
==PAGE:126==
==PAGE:127==
Chapter 12
Conclusion
The main objectives described in the problem statement were formulated as:
• Develop a software platform intended for visual inspection and manual annotation
of ICP signals.
• Compose a classiﬁcation protocol and description for unique ICP wave patterns.
• Use advanced machine learning on annotated ICP signals to promote an automated
ICP pattern recognition algorithm.
The software platform ICP-RC System was successfully developed and implemented in
collaboration with Clinic of Neurosurgery, Copenhagen University Hospital. The ICP-RC
System was used to manually annotate ICP patterns in 14 admitted patients. Hereof
were 8 patients scored by two neurologists independently. A spectrogram was introduced
as a visualization tool of the frequency content in the ICP signal, providing additional
information in ICP pattern assessment to the neurologists.
Six general ICP wave patterns were identiﬁed, described and exempliﬁed in a separate
protocol for ICP pattern recognition. The six classes are deﬁned from literature and clin-
ical experience adding a novel approach of ICP assessment. Identiﬁed classes are named
Solid for normal ICP pattern, Irregular for more ﬂuctuating appearance, also being a
new contribution to the ﬁeld of ICP waves. Regular B and A-waves already well-known
in literature are recognized as pathological patterns. Lastly deviation from A-waves is
described small A and Large A-Like waves - both being pathological. The pattern de-
scription includes mean ICP and amplitude variation as practiced in the Clinic. Further
development should include description of frequency content and appearance for each class.
Support Vector Machine was used to automatic recognize ICP pattern. Poor performance
was observed for ICP signals scored by both neurologists. Standardized and Raw annot-
ated ICP signals were classiﬁed to examine the inﬂuence on performance. No signiﬁcant
diﬀerence was found for F1score (p = 0.67) and accuracy (p = 0.051) between Stand-
ardized and Raw. However, the low p-values for accuracy indicates that standardizing
ICP signals before performing automatic classiﬁcation could be advantageous. Analysis of
interscore variability between neurologists revealed an agreement of 66.6 ± 10.2%, which
must be greatly improved before another machine learning should be attempted. The
Irregular class is identiﬁed to be to weakly deﬁned in both classiﬁcation protocol and in
clinical practice and calls for further research. Moreover, merging of multiple represented
A class should be considered for further consistency.

A.1.1
Kurtosis and Skewness
Kurtosis and skewness is a measure of data distribution in the epoch.
Kurtosis is a
measure of how outlier-prone a distribution is or visually how broad/slim a distribution
is as illustrated in ﬁgure A.1. The kurtosis computed by integrated MATLAB function1
given by.
kur = E(x −µ)4
σ4
(A.6)

Where µ is the mean of x and σ is the standard deviation. E(·) denotes the expected

skew = E(x −µ)3
σ3
(A.7)
If skewness is negative, the data is concentrated more to the left of the mean than to the
right and versa virsa for positive skewness. The skewness of the normal distribution (or
any perfectly symmetric distribution) is zero as illustrated in ﬁgure A.2.
A.1.2
Entropy
Entropy is based on information theory and is also used in pattern recognition.
Lets
consider a discrete random variable x, the question is how much information is received
when a speciﬁc value from x is observed. The amount of information can be viewed as the
“degree of surprise” on learning the value of x. The basic idea is, if the observed value x
is highly improbable more information is gained than observing a value that is very likely
to occur. The Entropy (H(x)) builds on the probability distribution of x (p(x)) and is
1http://se.mathworks.com/help/stats/kurtosis.html?s_tid=srchtitle
==PAGE:137==
A.1. TIME DOMAIN
121
computed by [6].
H(x) = −
X
x
p(x)log2(p(x))
(A.8)
Where the minus sign ensure the entropy to be positive or zero. Eq. A.8 is known as
the Shannon Entropy. A more general Entropy was provided by A. Renyi [44] where a
sensitivity term (α) to the distribution is introduced. The impact on the entropy can be
seen in ﬁgure A.3, where α →1 is a special case and becomes the Shannon Entropy and
is abbreviated “shanEnt” in table 7.1.
H(x) =
1
1 −αlog2
X
p(x)α
(A.9)
Figure A.3: Renyi’s entropy computed with diﬀerent α values. H1 is the shannon entropy.
Permutation Entropy described by Bandt et al. [5] arrange the data values into n equal
intervals of the total value range of the epoch. The number of values laying in that interval
is then assigned to a ﬁx constant k with k ∈Z between 1 and n, and put in to (p(π)). This
generates a probability of values appearing in a speciﬁc interval. The shannon entropy
(eq. A.8) is then taken on p(π). Figure A.4 shows a schematic illustration on the main
idea of permutation entropy.
Spectral Entropy described by H. Viertö-Oja et al. [15] describes the regularity (H =
0) or irregularity (H = 1) of the ICP signal compare to sine wave. The starting point for
the spectral entropy is by taking the Fourier transform of the epoch (X(fi)). The power
spectrum is acquired by squaring X(fi)
P(fi) = X(fi) · ¯X(fi)
where ¯X(fi) is the complex conjugate. The power spectrum is then normalized (Pn) so
the sum of the normalized power spectrum over the selected frequency region is equal to
one (ﬁgure A.5b and e). The spectral entropy is the computed using Shannons entropy
given in eq. A.8 as seen in ﬁgure A.5c and f.
S[fi, f2] =
f2
X
fi=f1
Pn(fi)log2(

Pn(fi)
(A.10)

shows the respective spectra. c) and f) illustrates the spectral entropy computed from

illustration purpose.
A.1.3
Hjorths Parameter
Hjorths parameters descibed by B. Hjorth [30] provides information about the, activity,
complexity and mobility of the ICP data within each epoch. Even though the parameters
are computed on time samples, Hjorths parameters also provides information about the
frequency content.
Activity = var(x(n))
(A.11)
The activity is the variance of the ICP epoch¨.
==PAGE:139==
A.1. TIME DOMAIN
123
Mobility =
s
var(x′(n))
var(x(n))
(A.12)
Mobility gives the ratio per time unit between the standard deviation of the slope with
reference to the standard deviation of the amplitude of the ICP. Where x′(n) represents
the 1’st derivative of the ICP signal. It can also be conceived as a mean frequency.
Complexity = mobility(x′(n))
mobility(x(n))
(A.13)
Complexity captures the excessive details with reference to the “softest” possible curve
shape, the sine wave, this corresponding to unity. It is expressed as the number of standard
slopes actually generated during the average time required for generation of one standard
amplitude as given by the mobility. This could capture repetitive patterns seen in the
regular B waves. Figure A.6 shows Hjorths illustration signal appearance of the three
parameters.
When looking at the ICP curves artifacts are present in terms of small
Figure A.6: Illustration taken from Hjorth [30]
duration of a sudden huge rise in amplitude for a few samples and then back the actual
ICP curve. Among the reasons for the artifacts are electrostatic discharge [4].
A.1.4
Lempel-Ziv Complexity
Lempel-Ziv’s complexity implemented by X. Zhang et al. [53] transforms the signal in to a
series of binary sequences by thresholding. A complexity counter (C(n)) is introduced to
count the number of distinct patterns in each sequence. To proper explain the algorithm
is a small example provide. A given sequence P is given as P = [001111000]. S and Q
are subsequences that contains part of the sequence P. Additional is SQ computed by
concatenating S and Q. Finally SQ* is found by removing the last sample in SQ. The
decision on a new subsequence is based on:
• if Q is a subsequence in SQ* then it is NOT a new subsequence
• if Q is NOT a subsequence in SQ* then it is a new subsequence
Table A.1 walks trough P step by step and provide the algorithm decision.
P is then sequenced as P = [0 · 01 · 1110 · 000] with the complexity count C(n) = 3. As
suggested by [26] is the threshold set to median of the epoch since this will capture the
activity no matter baseline and is less prone to outliers.

124

A.1.5
Slope Sign Change and Willison Amplitude
Slope Sign Change (SSC) is used by Englehart et al. [21] and Willison Amplitude (willi-
AMP) by Phinyomark et al. [41] on EMG signals. SSC is similar to Zero crossing only it
computes the number of changes between positive and negative slope among three consec-
utive segments xn−1, xn, xn+1. A threshold (th) needs to be determined to reduce noise.
The SSC is incremented if:
[xn > xn−1 ∧xn > xn+1]
∨
[xn < xn−1 ∧xn < xn+1]
and
|xn −xn+1| ≥td
∨
|xn −xn−1| ≥th
The Willison amplitude is the number of times that the diﬀerence between ICP signal
amplitude among two adjacent segments exceeds a predeﬁned threshold (th).
williAMP =
N−1
X
n=1
f(|xn −xn+1|)
(A.14)

(
1
if

0
otherwise
There is no suggestion to the best threshold for the Willison Amplitude since it needs to
be empirical set.

A.2. FREQUENCY ANALYSIS
125
A.2
Frequency Analysis
From the State Of The Art chapter, duration is described for both A- and B-waves. A-
waves are noted to have a duration of 5-20 min and continuous B-waves have 0.5 - 2 min
repeating it self. Combined with a sampling frequency of 5 Hz, a frequency analysis will
only reveal the frequency content of 0-2.5 Hz due to Nyquist sampling rule. As seen in
the ICP-RC system is it a way of providing extra information to the doctors. Taking the
Fourier Transform (FT) of the ICP signal will show the frequency content of the whole
ICP signal. However this will only reveal the overall frequency content of the signal and
will not be suﬃcient as a feature. Two diﬀerent methods of doing so are Short Term
Fourier Transform (STFT) and Wavelet transform.
A.2.1
Short Term Fourier Transform
STFT is one way the get more information of the frequency content in the ICP signal.
Instead of taking the FT of the whole signal the STFT split the ICP signal into smaller
successive windows containing a ﬁnite number of samples. The assumptions is that the
signal inside the window are quasistationary. The STFT is given by:
X[n, λ] =
∞
X
m=−∞
x[n + m]w[m]e−jλm
(A.15)
where w[m] is window m samples and λ is the frequency variable. The window should be
of type Hamming or similar to reduce ringings. Furthermore is it important to keep in
mind that performing a FT on small time-windows will result in a good time resolution
but poor frequency and vice versa. As illustrated in ﬁgure A.7, will this property always
be a trade oﬀand selecting the proper window length and frequency bands depends on
the application.
Figure A.7: STFT with two window lengths illustrating the consequence of window size.
Left) shows good time resolution and poor frequency. Center) shows trade-oﬀbetween
medicore time and frequency resolution. Right) shows poor time and good frequency
resolution.
A.2.2
Wavelet Transformation
As mentioned in above section is the trade oﬀbetween time and frequency resolution
in STFT a major drawback. To overcome this problem is Discrete Wavelet Transform
(DWT) a popular alternative. In stead of splitting the windows up in equal size, it is
split up accordingly to the frequency of interest. That is, long windows in time for low
frequency components and small windows for high frequency components as illustrated in
ﬁgure A.8.
==PAGE:142==
126
APPENDIX A. FEATURE EQUATION REFERENCE PAGES
Figure A.8: schematic illustration of Wavelet transformation. Shows varying time windows
ﬁt appropriate frequency of interest.
DWT uses a diﬀerent set of basis functions called wavelets and are created by shifting,
expanding and contracting a single wavelet called the mother wavelet A wavelet function
is deﬁned as [1]:
DWT[a, b] =
1
√a
N−1
X
n=−∞
x[n]ψ
n −b
a

(A.16)
Where x[n] is the ICP signal, ψ is the mother wavelet, a = 2j is the scaling factor and
b = k2j is a shifting term in time with j, k ∈Z.
1
√a is a normalization term to ensure
that the energy stays in the same level for diﬀerent a and b values. The power of 2 is for
computational convenience.
The DWT is implemented in the MATLAB function wavedec that uses an eﬀective
way developed by Mallet et al. [38]. The method breaks the signal in to an approximation
band (ai) and a detail band (di) by a linear combination of low pass and high pass ﬁltering
and can be witten as:
DWT(t) =
∞
X
k=−∞
ckφ(t −k) +
∞
X
k=−∞
∞
X
j=−∞
dj,kψ(2jt −k)
(A.17)
where φ(.) is the scaling function, dj,k are the wavelet coeﬃcients, and ck are the scaling
coeﬃcients. The ﬁrst term in Eq. A.17 represents an approximation to the general trend of
the original function. The second term represents the local details in the original function.
The DWT ﬁlter algorithm constructs a high pass ﬁlter corresponding to the wave-
let function ψj,k(t) maintaining the signal details and a low pass ﬁlter corresponding to
the scale function φj,k(t) represents the trend of the approximation to the signal. In the
implementation, the signal is convolved with the high pass ﬁlter to produce the detailed
wavelet coeﬃcients and the low pass ﬁlter to produce the shape approximating scale coef-
ﬁcients [1]. The output bands have half the frequency bandwidth of the original signal
and is downsampled by two according to Nyquist rule. The procedure is repeated on the
approximation band to the desired level of decompositions. The procedure is illustrated
in ﬁgure A.9.
The choice of wavelets depends on the signal, however Daubechies wavelets are com-
monly used and is reported to do well in EEG [29]. The frequency interval of the DWT
on the ICP signal with fs = 5
Hz are summarized in table A.2.
A.2.3
Teager Energy Operator
Teager Energy Operator (TEO) described by Erdamar et al. [22] is computed on the
ﬁrst four frequency band. The TEO output increases the instantaneous changes such as
==PAGE:143==
A.2. FREQUENCY ANALYSIS
127
Figure A.9: Input signal x[n] is ﬁltered by a low and high pass ﬁlter to acquired the
approximation (ai) and detail (di) band. The frequency bandwidth is half for each de-
composition level. The signal is downsampled by 2 in order to comply with the Nyquist
rule.
Table A.2: Summary of the frequencies for the detail band at each decomposition level
with fs = 5Hz.

discontinuities, increase or decrease of amplitude and frequency in the signal. Furthermore
does TEO suppress the soft transitions and background activity. TEO in discrete is deﬁned
by:
ψTs(n) = ψ2
s(n) −ψs(n −1)ψs(n + 1)
(A.18)
where ψs(n) and ψTs(n) are described as the n’th sample value of the signal and the TEO

A.2.4
Spectral Centroid and Roll-oﬀ
Two alternative frequency measures commonly used in speech recognition are the spectral
centroid (eq. A.19 and roll-oﬀ(eq. A.20 [49].

PN−1
m=0 m|Xi(m)|
PN−1
m=0 |Xi(m)|
(A.19)
With |Xi(m)| is the magnitude of the FT. The spectral centroid is a measure of the spectral
shape. High values corresponds to higher structures with more energy in the frequencies.
mR

c
100
N−1
X

|Xi(m)|
(A.20)
c is a constant (e.g. c = 85 or 90) of the maximum magnitude distribution of the DFT
coeﬃcients must be concentrated.
The roll-oﬀis a measure indicating where most of
the spectral energy is concentrated. It is a measure of skewness of the spectral shape.
Right-skewed shapes results in higher values.

ConvertToMat.m
% % This script opens large txt files in parts and saves it as a mat file.
% Get complete file name
filename = [PathName FileName];
% Start process bar til inform user on progress
progress = 0;
wb
= waitbar(progress, 'Scanning file...');
% Read File
fileID
= fopen(filename,'r');
% Initialize file size counter
count
= 0;
fs
= 100; %[Hz]
% Count file size by count line for line
while ischar(fgetl(fileID))
count = count +1;
end
% compute number of chunks
t
= 3;
%[hours]
fileStep = round(count/(t*fs*3600));
% Split into t hour chunks with fs
% Update progress bar
progress = 0.3;
wb = waitbar(progress, wb,'Converting file...');
% Load the current chunk an get start and end row of that chunk in the txt
% file into temporary variables and save them as mat.
for i= 1:fileStep
% Compute start and end location for chunk in file
% start line 2 because of header
startRow
= (i-1)*(t*100*3600)+2;
endRow
= i*(t*100*3600)+1;
% Get Date, Time and ICP curve for chunk
[Date, Time, ICP]= loadnPartData(filename, startRow, endRow);
% Resample ICP from 100 Hz down to 5 Hz
ICP
= resample(ICP, 5,100);
ICP
= num2cell(ICP);

B.2.2
EraseAnn.m
function handlesOut = EraseAnn(handles)
% Erase user marked annotation from ICP graph in main UI
% INPUT
handles:
ADC with ICP and loaded patient.
%
Annotation list can be empty or have annotation
%
values
%
% OUTPUT
handlesOut: ADC with updated annotation list
%
% Author: Asger J. M.
% Ask for User input
[ErX ErY]
= ginput(1);
% Get annotation list with start, end values and labels
if ~isempty(handles.ADC.annotation{:,1})
xStart
= handles.ADC.annotation{:,1};
xEnd
= handles.ADC.annotation{:,2};
annLabel
= handles.ADC.annotation{:,3};
end
% compute the difference between user given point and annotation list
distXStart
= abs(xStart - ErX);
distXEnd
= abs(xEnd - ErX);
% Find lowest difference of start and end row.
[rStart , ~]
= find(distXStart == min(distXStart));
[rEnd , ~]
= find(distXEnd == min(distXEnd));
% if start and end row are equal then remove from list
if rStart == rEnd
xStart(rStart)
= [];
% remove ientified start value
xEnd(rEnd)
= [];
% remove identified end value
annLabel(rEnd,:)
= [];
% remove identified class label
else
% Look at the second shortest difference
sortStart
= sort(distXStart);
sortEnd
= sort(distXEnd);
[rStart_new , ~]
= find(distXStart == sortStart(2));
[rEnd_new , ~]
= find(distXEnd == sortEnd(2));
% remove 1st row from start and 2nd from end if these are equal
if rStart == rEnd_new
xStart(rStart)
= [];
xEnd(rEnd_new)
= [];
annLabel(rEnd_new,:)= [];
% remove 2nd row from start and 1st from end if these are equal
elseif rEnd == rStart_new
xStart(rStart_new)
= [];
xEnd(rEnd)
= [];
annLabel(rEnd,:)
= [];
end
end
==PAGE:155==
B.2. HELP FUNCTIONS
139
% Update annotation list
handles.ADC.annotation
= {xStart, xEnd, annLabel};
handlesOut
= handles;
end

function handlesOut = EraseLastAnn(handles)

ADC with ICP and loaded patient.
%
Annotation list can be empty or have annotation
%
values
%

%
% Author: Asger J. M.

if ~isempty(handles.ADC.annotation{:,1})

= [];
handles.ADC.annotation{:,2}(end)
= [];
handles.ADC.annotation{:,3}(end,:)
= [];
end

B.2.4
ﬁndFs.m
function [ fs ] = findFs( data )
% Takes first 100 lines of txt file and convert the milisecond stamp to
% numeric values. The difference time detmines the Fs, which only can be
% 100 or 5 Hz.
%
%INPUT
data: 100 lines from the ICP data time stamp as string
%
%OUTPUT
fs: sample frequency of loaded patient file. can either be 5 or
%
100 Hz.
%
%Author: Asger J. M.
% Allocating for speed
dataFs = zeros(100,1);
% Start at 2 due to header of txt file
% difference is taken between the milisecsnd stamps, if the difference is
% less than 1/50 if a sec then fs = 100 if not then fs = 5 Hz.
for i = 2:101
temp = data{i,1}(9:end);
% get current line
dataFs(i) = str2num(temp(end-3:end));
% take milisec values
dtime = diff(dataFs);
% compute difference
mdtime = mean(dtime);
% mean if some NaN are present
% determine if fs is 100 or 5 Hz
if mdtime < (1/50)
fs = 100;
else
fs = 5;
end
end
